Ruling on Backgammon and Chess in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling with inter-madhhab dispute and proofs from Quran and Hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on chess (with and without gambling), identify the proofs from Quran and hadith, and explain the disagreement with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Chess is haram in the Hanafi school even without gambling because it is idle play and diverts from worship.

This passage addresses two games: backgammon and chess. While backgammon is unequivocally condemned, chess has a well-known inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi school prohibits chess outright, while al-Shafi'i permits it without stakes. We will examine the Hanafi proofs and the reasons for the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two games and their rulings
Determine the ruling for backgammon (nardashir) from the strong simile, and for chess (shatranj) both with and without gambling.
Backgammon is likened to dipping hand in pork blood – severe prohibition.
2
Locate the consensus on gambling
Note that chess with gambling (qimar) is haram by consensus, as all schools agree on the prohibition of gambling.
Gambling is haram by clear Quranic text.
3
Understand the Hanafi proof for prohibition without gambling
The text cites Quran 23:115 (creation not for play), narrations from Ali and Ibn Abbas likening chess to idolatry, and a hadith cursing the player. These form the Hanafi dalil.
The key concept is 'abath (idle play) and being lahw other than the three permitted amusements.
4
Compare with al-Shafi'i's position
Al-Shafi'i differs, considering chess permissible if no gambling. The Hanafi response is that it is still haram because of its nature.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different interpretations of the same texts.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on greeting
Abu Hanifa allows greeting a chess player; Abu Yusuf dislikes it as humiliation. This shows nuance within the school.
This does not affect the ruling of playing itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من لعب بالنردشير فكأنما غمس يده في لحم الخنزير ودمه
(1)
وأما الشطرنج ؛ فإن قامر به فهو حرام أيضاً بالإجماع ؛ لأن الله تعالى حرم القمار، وإن لم يقامر به فكذلك عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي له، ودليله مع جوابنا عنه مذكور في الشرح إجمالاً، وتفصيلُ الجواب الذي هو حجتنا عليه حقيقة أنه عبث وهو حرام ؛ لقوله : أَفَحَسِبْتُمْ أَنَّمَا خَلَقْنَتَكُمْ عَبَنا ) .
وأيضاً : إنه لهو سوى الثلاث التي ذكرها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، فيكون حراماً، وتما يدل على الحرمة صريحاً ما روى عن علي له أنه مرّ بقوم يلعبون بالشطرنج فقال : ما هذه التماثيل التي أنتم لها عاكفون، وروى مثل هذا عن ابن . ه حين مرّ بقوم يلعبون به وقد شبه عملهم بعمل عبدة الأصنام.
وقال في «الكشاف»: وعن علي : النرد والشطرنج من الميسر، وأيضاً : إنّه لهو يصد صاحبه عن الجمع والجماعة، وهل رأيت صاحبه يصلي فضلاً عن الجماعة، فمن أباحه فقد أعانَ الشيطان على الإسلام والمسلمين ، وقد أورد الإمام أبو موسى له في كتاب الأمالي بإسناده إلى حيّة بن مسلم أنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «ملعون من يلعب بالشطرنج، والناظر إليها كاكل لحم الخنزير».
ثم اللاعب به إن قامر لم تقبل شهادته، وإلا تقبل ؛ لأنه متأوّل، ولا بأس بالسلام عليه عند الأعظم الله له حتى . يشغله عما هو فيه، وكره أبو يوسف له ذلك إهانة له. هذا زبدة ما في «النهاية» (۳).
Whoever plays backgammon, it is as if he immersed his hand in the flesh and blood of swine. As for chess, if he plays for stakes, it is forbidden by consensus because Allah forbade gambling. If he does not play for stakes, it is likewise forbidden in our school, contrary to al-Shafi'i. His proof and our response are mentioned in the commentary in summary. The detailed response which is our argument against him is that it is idle play (abath) and it is haram because of His saying: 'Did you think that We created you in play?' (23:115). Also it is amusement (lahw) other than the three which the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) mentioned, so it is haram. And what proves the prohibition explicitly is what was narrated from ‘Ali (ﷺ) that he passed by a group playing chess and said: 'What are these images to which you are devoted?' And similar was narrated from Ibn ‘Abbas (ﷺ) when he passed by people playing chess and likened their act to the worship of idols. And it is said in al-Kashshaf: from ‘Ali: backgammon and chess are gambling (al-maysir). Also it is amusement that distracts one from the Friday prayer and congregation. Have you seen a chess player pray, let alone attend congregation? So whoever permits it has assisted Satan against Islam and Muslims. Imam Abu Musa (ﷺ) narrated in his book al-Amalī with his chain to Hayya ibn Muslim that he said: The Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) said: 'Cursed is the one who plays chess, and the one who looks at it is like one who eats the flesh of swine.' Then the player: if he gambles, his testimony is not accepted; otherwise it is accepted because he is a muta’awwil (one with a valid interpretation). There is no harm in greeting him according to al-A’zam (Abu Hanifa), so as not to distract him from what he is doing. Abu Yusuf disliked that as a form of humiliation. This is the essence of what is in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on two games: backgammon (nardashir) and chess (shatranj). For backgammon, it states that anyone who plays it is as if he dipped his hand in pork flesh and blood, indicating severe prohibition. For chess, the ruling is divided: if played with gambling (qimar), it is haram by consensus. If played without gambling, the Hanafi school holds it is still haram, while al-Shafi'i permits it. The Hanafi proof is that chess is idle play (abath) and the Quran condemns play and amusement. The verse from Surah al-Mu’minun (23:115) is cited. Additionally, it is a form of lahwi (amusement) other than the three permitted: playing with one's wife, training horses, and archery (according to a hadith). The text also mentions narrations from ‘Ali and Ibn ‘Abbas condemning chess, and a hadith cursing the player. The passage then addresses the legal consequences: if the player gambles, his testimony (shahada) is rejected; if not, it is accepted because he follows a possible interpretation (muta’awwil) that it might be permissible? Actually the Hanafi position is that it is haram but some may be under the impression it is permissible, so their testimony is accepted if they don't gamble. Finally, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute on greeting a chess player: Abu Hanifa allows it to avoid distracting him, while Abu Yusuf dislikes it as a form of humiliation.

Key Terms
الشطرنج (al-shatranj): Chess
النردشير (al-nardashir): Backgammon
القمار (al-qimar): Gambling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on playing chess without gambling?
A) Halal
B) Makruh
C) Haram
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, chess is haram even without gambling because it is idle play and a distraction from worship, based on Quranic principle and prophetic and companion traditions.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Collar on a Slave and Selling Mecca Land
Intra-Hanafi dispute on two specific prohibitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on placing a collar on a slave and selling land in Mecca, explain the rationale for each, and identify how custom can alter a ruling.

Core Idea
The ruling on Mecca land and slave collars differs between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn; custom can shift a ruling from disliked to permissible.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on two distinct issues: placing an iron collar on a slave and transactions involving land in Mecca. While Abu Hanifa prohibits both, his two principal students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, permit them. The commentary also highlights that the ruling on the collar changed over time due to custom, illustrating the dynamic nature of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues
The passage addresses two separate rulings: (1) placing an iron collar around a slave's neck, and (2) selling or renting land in Mecca.
Both issues are discussed together due to the same underlying dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and reasoning
Abu Hanifa prohibits both actions because he considers Mecca's land sacred (haram) and not subject to ownership, and the collar is degrading unless permitted by custom.
For Abu Hanifa, the sacredness of Mecca prevents any sales or leases of land.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani permit both actions because they consider Mecca's land owned (mamlukah) by its inhabitants, and the collar is allowed if it is customary.
The Sahibayn give weight to ownership rights and local customs.
4
Note the role of custom regarding the collar
The commentary explains that in earlier times, collars were rare and thus disliked (makruh), but later, due to an increase in runaway slaves, the practice became common and thus permissible.
This shows that custom can change the ruling even within the same school.
5
Understand the practical consequence
The difference leads to a thamarah al-khilaf: followers of Abu Hanifa avoid these actions, while followers of the Sahibayn permit them. The mufta bihi may depend on the prevalence of custom.
The commentator suggests that in his time, the collar is no longer disliked due to custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجعلُ الغُل في عنق عبده، وبيع أرض مكة وإجارتها)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ؛ لأن مكة ،حرام وعندهما: يجوز لأن أرضها مملوكة.
[1]قوله : وجعل الغل...الخ ؛ أي كرة أيضاً أن يجعل في عنق عبده طوقاً مسمراً بسمار عظيم يمنعه عن تحريك رأسه، هو معتاد بين الظلمة، قال في «النهاية»: الغُلّ: علامة يعلم بها أنه أبق، ولا بأس به في زماننا بغلبة الآبق سيما في اليهود، وكان هذا في زمانهم مكروهاً لقلته قاله الجلبي الله (۲)
And placing the iron collar around the neck of his slave, and selling the land of Mecca and renting it: this according to Abu Hanifa because Mecca is sacred, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is permissible because its land is owned. [1] His saying: 'placing the collar... etc.': that is, it is also disliked to place around the neck of his slave a studded collar with a large nail that prevents him from moving his head, which is customary among oppressors. It is said in 'al-Nihaya': the collar is a mark by which it is known that he is a fugitive (abeq), and there is no harm in it in our time due to the prevalence of fugitives especially among the Jews, and it was disliked in their time due to its rarity, as stated by al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding two separate rulings. The first ruling is about placing an iron collar (ghull) around a slave's neck, which is a degrading punishment. The second is about selling or renting land in Mecca. Abu Hanifa prohibits both because he considers Mecca's land sacred (haram) and thus not subject to ownership transactions. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) permit them because they hold that the land of Mecca is owned (mamlukah) by its inhabitants, so transactions are valid. The commentator explains that the collar is a specific type with a nail that restricts movement, which was a common practice of oppressors. He notes that in the time of the earlier scholars, such collars were rare and thus disliked (makruh), but in later times (the commentator's time), they became more common due to many slaves escaping, so it is no longer disliked, especially regarding Jewish slaves. This reflects the principle of urf (custom) affecting rulings.

Key Terms
الغُل (al-ghull): A metal collar placed around a slave's neck as a mark or punishment
الحرم (al-haram): The sacred precinct of Mecca

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does Abu Hanifa prohibit selling land in Mecca?
A) Because it is owned by non-Muslims
B) Because Mecca's land is sacred (haram)
C) Because the Prophet forbade it
D) Because it leads to dispute

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, differences between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn exist on the permissibility of selling Mecca land and placing collars, with custom playing a decisive role in the latter.

Ruling on Saying 'Bi-Ma'qidi al-'Izzi min 'Arshik'
Intra-Hanafi dispute on supplication phrase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the phrase 'bi-ma'qidi al-'izzi min 'arshik' and evaluate the evidence for each position.

Core Idea
Even a prophetic supplication may be avoided if it conflicts with a definitive principle of aqeedah.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses a controversial phrase used in supplication: 'bi-ma'qidi al-'izzi min 'arshik'. While it appears in a narrated hadith, the Hanafi masters disagree on its permissibility due to theological implications. Let's examine the arguments and the preferred ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed phrase
Locate the phrase 'bi-ma'qidi al-'izzi min 'arshik' in the passage. It is part of a longer supplication narrated from Qaylah.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They hold the phrase is problematic (makruh tahrimi) because it suggests that Allah's attribute of honor is attached to the Throne, a created entity, implying that honor is created—contradicting Allah's eternal attribute.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf and Abu al-Layth permit the phrase because it is reported in a valid hadith.
4
Analyze the evidence for each
Abu Hanifa's argument is based on a definitive (qat'i) principle of aqeedah: Allah's attributes are not created nor attached to creation. Abu Yusuf relies on a khabar wahid. The commentator notes that a khabar wahid cannot override a qat'i principle.
5
Determine the tarjih
The commentator favors avoidance (ahwat) because the hadith is a single report and contradicts a certain principle. This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله في دعائه : بمعقدِ العزّ من عرشك ، وبحق رسلك وأنبيائك) ؛ لأنَّه يوهم تعلق عزّه بالعرش، ولا حق لأحدٍ على الله له، وعند أبي يوسف له : يجوز الأول للدعاء المأثور
[1] قوله : بمعقد العز...الخ ؛ بتقديم العين وتأخير القاف من العقد، وهو المعروف في هذا الدعاء، وفيه الخلاف المذكور بينهما، وبين أبي يوسف له. ووجه قولهما : إنّه توهم تعلق عزّه بالعرش، وإنّ عزّه حادث، لتعلقه بالحادث، والله تعالى عزيز لم يزل ولا يزال، وعزّه صفةٌ لم تزل ولا تزال موصوفاً به، ووجه قول الثاني، وبه قال أبو الليث الله : لا بأس به ، لما روي أنه كان دعائه، والأحوط الامتناع لكونه خبر واحد، يخالف القطع.
(۱) عن قيلة بنت مخرمة رضي الله عنها كانت إذا أخذت حظها من المضجع بعد العتمة ، قالت : بسم الله وأتوكل على الله، وضعت جنبي لربي واستغفره لذنبي، حتى تقولها مراراً، ثم تقول : أعوذ بالله وبكلماته التامات التي لا يجاوزهن بر ولا فاجر من شر ما يُنزل من السماء وما يعرج فيها، وشر ما يُنزل في الأرض وشرّ ما يخرج منها ، وشر فتن النهار وشرّ طوارق الليل الا طارقا يطرق بخير، آمنت بالله واعتصمت به الحمد لله الذي استسلم لقدرته كل شيء، والحمد لله الذي ذل لعزّته كل شيء، والحمد لله الذي تواضع لعظمته كل شيء، والحمد لله الذي خشع لملكه كل شيء، اللهم اني أسألك بمعاقد العزّ من عرشك، ومنتهى الرحمة من كتابك وجدك الأعلى واسمك الأكبر وكلماتك التامات التي لا يجاوزهن بر ولا فاجر أن تنظر إلينا نظرة مرحومة، لا تدع لنا ذنبا إلا غفرته، ولا فقراً إلا جبرته ولا عدواً إلا أهلكته ولا عرياناً إلا كسوته ولا ديناً إلا قضيته ولا أمراً لنا فيه صلاح في الدنيا والآخرة إلا أعطيتناه يا أرحم الراحمين آمنت بالله ، واعتصمت به ثم تقول : سبحان الله ثلاثا وثلاثين والله أكبر ثلاثاً وثلاثين، والحمد لله أربعاً وثلاثين، ثم تقول يا بنتي هذه رأس الخاتمة إن بنت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أتته تستخدمه، فقال: ألا أدلك على خير من خادم قالت بلى فأمرها بهذه المئة عند المضجع بعد العتمة. في «المعجم الكبير (٢٥ : ١٢) ، قال الهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد ۱۰ : ١٢٥): إسناده حسن. (۲) في «ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٨٥).
And his saying in his supplication: 'By the binding place of honor from Your Throne, and by the right of Your Messengers and Your Prophets' — because it gives the impression that His honor is attached to the Throne, and no one has any right upon Allah. According to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him): the first (phrase) is permissible due to the reported supplication. [1] His saying: 'by the binding place of honor... etc.' — with the ‘ayn before the qaf from 'aqd, which is well-known in this supplication. There is the aforementioned disagreement between the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf. The face of their (the two's) view: it gives the impression that His honor is attached to the Throne, and that His honor is created because it is attached to a created thing, while Allah is Mighty (aziz) eternally and His honor is an attribute that has always been His description. The face of the second view, which is also the view of Abu al-Layth (may Allah have mercy on him): there is no harm in it because it has been narrated that it was his (the Prophet's) supplication. And the more cautious (ahwat) is to abstain because it is a single report (khabar wahid) that contradicts a definitive (qat'i) principle. (1) From Qaylah bint Makhramah (may Allah be pleased with her) ... [the hadith follows].
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific phrase used in supplication: 'bi-ma‘qidi al-‘izzati min ‘arshik' (by the binding place of honor from Your Throne). The issue is whether it is permissible to say this in prayer or general supplication. Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the two) consider it problematic (makruh or haram) because it gives the impression that Allah's attribute of honor (izzah) is attached to the Throne, which is a created entity. This would imply that honor is created and that Allah is not eternally described with it. Abu Yusuf allows it because it is found in a reported supplication of the Prophet (ﷺ) or a righteous woman (Qaylah). The commentator notes that the more cautious position is to avoid it because the proof is a khabar wahid (single-narrator hadith) which contradicts the definitive principle (qat'i) of Allah's transcendence. The passage then quotes a long hadith from Qaylah which includes the phrase 'bi-ma‘qidi al-‘izzi min ‘arshik' as part of a longer supplication. This serves as evidence for Abu Yusuf's position. The commentary thus presents an intra-Hanafi dispute with a clear tarjih (preference) for avoidance.

Key Terms
معقد العز (ma'qid al-'izz): The binding place or source of honor, used in supplication
خبر واحد (khabar wahid): A hadith with single narrator chain, not mutawatir
الأحوط (al-ahwat): The more precautionary position

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider the phrase 'bi-ma'qidi al-'izzi min 'arshik' problematic?
A) Because it is not in Arabic
B) Because it implies Allah's honor is attached to a created thing
C) Because it is too long
D) Because it uses the word 'arsh which is forbidden

Key Takeaway
Even a prophetic supplication narration (khabar wahid) cannot override a definitive aqeedah principle, so the Hanafi position avoids this phrase.

Ruling on Saying 'Bi Ma'qid al-'Izz' in Supplication
Intra-Hanafi dispute on a supplication phrase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using the phrase 'bi ma'qid al-'izz min 'arshik' in supplication and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A supplication phrase that implies a location for Allah's attribute is avoided out of caution for tawhid.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, we encounter an intra-Hanafi dispute: is it permissible to say 'by the knot of might from Your Throne' in supplication? While the wording appears in a prophetic hadith, the majority of Hanafi scholars deem it makruh due to its implication that Allah's might is attached to the Throne, a created thing. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of both sides and the principle of caution when definitive beliefs are at stake.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific act under dispute? Identify the phrase and state the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
The act is saying 'bi ma'qid al-'izz min 'arshik' in supplication. Their ruling: makruh tanzihi.
2
State the Hanafi position of the two
Explain the reasoning behind their ruling: the phrase suggests that Allah's attribute of might is attached to the Throne, a created entity, implying a location and temporality for the attribute.
This contradicts the eternal, uncreated nature of Allah's attributes.
3
Identify the opponent's view
Abu Yusuf (and Abu al-Layth) permit the phrase. What is their evidence?
They rely on the hadith of Qaylah bint Makhramah which contains the exact phrase.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How do the two scholars rebut Abu Yusuf's evidence? They argue that the hadith is a khabar wahid (solitary report) that conflicts with the qat'i principle of tanzih (transcendence).
In Hanafi usul, a solitary report cannot override a definitive theological principle.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
What is the relied-upon position for fatwa? The text says 'al-ahwat al-imtina'' (caution is to refrain), so the mufta bihi is makruh.
Although Abu Yusuf's view is respected, the majority view prevails due to the strength of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية / الأكل والشرب واللبس وغيرها
وقوله في دعائه: بمعقدِ العزّ من عرشك، وبحق رسلك وأنبيائك
[١]
(وقوله في دعائه : بمعقدِ العزّ من عرشك ، وبحق رسلك وأنبيائك) ؛ لأنَّه يوهم تعلق عزّه بالعرش، ولا حق لأحدٍ على الله له، وعند أبي يوسف له : يجوز الأول للدعاء المأثور
،

(۲) في «ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٨٥).
Book of Disliked Acts / Eating, Drinking, Dressing, etc. And his statement in his supplication: 'By the knot of might from Your Throne, and by the right of Your messengers and prophets.' [1] (His statement in his supplication: 'By the knot of might from Your Throne, and by the right of Your messengers and prophets') because it suggests that His might is attached to the Throne, and no one has a right against Allah. According to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him: the first is permissible due to the reported supplication. [2] A mark by which it is known that he is a runaway; there is no harm in it in our time due to the prevalence of runaways, especially among the Jews, but it was disliked in their time due to its scarcity, said al-Jalabi. [1] Commentary on 'bi ma'qid al-'izz...': with the 'ayn before and the qaf after from 'aqd (knot), and this is the well-known wording in this supplication. There is the mentioned disagreement between them and Abu Yusuf. The reasoning of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): it suggests that His might is attached to the Throne, and that His might is created because it is attached to a created thing, while Allah Almighty is Ever-Mighty and has not ceased to be, and His attribute of might has not ceased and He has never ceased to be described by it. The reasoning of the second, and Abu al-Layth said: there is no harm in it because it was reported that it was his supplication, but caution is to refrain because it is a solitary report that contradicts definitive proof. [Hadith from Qaylah bint Makhramah] ... narrated in al-Mu'jam al-Kabir, Al-Haythami said its chain is good.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of using the phrase 'bi ma'qid al-'izz min 'arshik' in supplication. The author identifies the issue: the phrase may imply that Allah's attribute of might ('izz) is attached to the Throne, which is a created entity, thus suggesting that the attribute itself is created. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two), who consider it makruh. Abu Yusuf, however, permits it on the basis of a reported supplication from the Prophet (peace be upon him) via Qaylah bint Makhramah. The commentary explains that the word 'ma'qid' is derived from 'aqd (knot), and the phrase is well-known. The two scholars argue that using such wording creates a mistaken belief that Allah's might is located in a specific place, which contradicts the eternal, uncreated nature of His attributes. Abu Yusuf relies on the hadith evidence, but the author notes that caution (al-ahwat) is to refrain, as the hadith is a khabar wahid (solitary report) that conflicts with a definitive principle. The passage includes the full text of the hadith as quoted from Qaylah, which contains the same phrase. Thus the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that such wording is makruh due to the danger of tashbih (anthropomorphism).

Key Terms
بمعقد العز (bi ma'qid al-'izz): A phrase in supplication meaning 'by the knot of might from Your Throne'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider the phrase makruh?
A) It is not mentioned in the Quran.
B) It implies Allah's might is attached to the Throne.
C) It contains a grammatical error.
D) It is an innovation in worship.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers caution over a solitary report when theological purity is at stake; therefore, avoid phrasing that suggests any location for Allah's attributes.

Ruling on Tashir and Naqt of the Quran
Ruling on adding markers to the Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adding tenth-markers and dots to the Quran and identify who it is recommended for.

Core Idea
Adding markers and dots to the Quran is good for non-Arabs; for Arabs it is not disliked.

This passage addresses a practical question: is it permissible to add markings to the Qur'anic text? The Hanafi school distinguishes between those who need such aids and those who do not. We will examine the ruling, its rationale, and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts
The acts are tashir (adding markers every ten verses) and naqt (dotting the letters). These are practical aids for recitation.
Tashir and naqt are not part of the original text but later developments.
2
Determine the ruling for Araabs
For native Arabic speakers (Arabs), the text implies that tashir and naqt are permissible but not encouraged, as they are not needed.
The ruling is based on the absence of prohibition and the principle of ibaha.
3
Determine the ruling for non-Arabs
For non-Arabs, tashir and naqt are considered 'hasan' (good) because they help in correct recitation.
This is a concession (rukhsah) to facilitate learning.
4
Understand the reasoning
The underlying cause is need: non-Arabs require such aids to recite accurately, while Arabs do not. The ruling adapts to benefit.
This shows the Hanafi consideration of 'illa and maslahah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
وتعشير المصحف ونقطه إلا للعجم، فإنَّهُ حسن لهم، واحتكار قوتِ البشر والبهائم في بلد يضر بأهله
[٢]
(وتعشير) المصحف ونقطه إلا للعجم، فإنَّهُ حسن لهم" (۲۱)، واحتكار قوتِ البشر والبهائم في بلد يضر بأهلِهِ ، التَّخصيص بالقوتِ قول أبي حنيفة وعند أبي يوسف : كل ما أضرَّ بالعامَّةِ حبسه فهو احتكار، وعن محمد ه : لا احتكار في النياب)
Husn al-Dirayah for the end of Sharh al-Wiqayah. And adding tenth-markers (tashir) to the Mushaf and its dotting (naqt) except for non-Arabs, for it is good for them. And hoarding the sustenance of humans and animals in a city that harms its people. [2] (Tashir) of the Mushaf and its dotting except for non-Arabs, for it is good for them. And hoarding the sustenance of humans and animals in a city that harms its people. Restriction to sustenance (qut) is the saying of Abu Hanifa, and according to Abu Yusuf: everything that harms the general public when detained is hoarding. And from Muhammad: no hoarding in cloth.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings. First, regarding tashir (adding markers every ten verses) and naqt (dotting) of the Quran: it is not disliked except for non-Arabs who need it, for whom it is considered good (hasan). Second, regarding ihtikar (hoarding): hoarding the staple food (qut) of humans and animals is makruh when it harms the people of the city. The author presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the definition of hoarding. Abu Hanifa restricts the prohibition to staple food (qut). Abu Yusuf expands it to any commodity whose detention harms the public. Muhammad al-Shaybani adds that there is no hoarding in cloth (i.e., clothing is not subject to hoarding rules). The proof for the ruling on hoarding is the hadith: 'The importer is blessed, and the hoarder is cursed' (Ibn Majah). The underlying reason is that hoarding harms the public by creating scarcity.

Key Terms
تعشير (tashir): Adding markers or symbols every ten verses in the Quran.
نقط (naqt): Dotting letters to distinguish similar letters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on adding tenth-markers (tashir) to the Quran for non-Arabs?
A) Haram (forbidden)
B) Makruh (disliked)
C) Hasan (good)
D) Mubah (neutral)

Key Takeaway
Tashir and naqt are permitted, and for non-Arabs they are recommended as aids for correct recitation.

Ruling on Hoarding (Ihtikar) – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on hoarding with intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on hoarding, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and apply the practical consequence of their ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Hoarding staple food that harms the public is makruh, but its scope differs among the Hanafi imams.

This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on ihtikar (hoarding) along with an intra-madhhab dispute. The basic ruling is that hoarding the staple food of humans and animals in a place where it harms the people is makruh. However, the definition of 'what counts as hoarding' varies among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding their positions helps us apply the ruling in different market scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its basic ruling
The act is ihtikar – hoarding goods to wait for a price increase. The basic ruling is makruh tanzihi (disliked) when it harms the people of the city.
Ruling may become haram if severe harm results.
2
Understand the three Hanafi positions on scope
Abu Hanifa restricts the prohibition to staple food (qut) of humans and animals. Abu Yusuf extends it to any commodity whose detention harms the public. Muhammad al-Shaybani adds that there is no hoarding in cloth (i.e., clothing is not subject to ihtikar).
The dispute centers on the effective cause ('illa): is it the type of good (qut) or the harm caused?
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, hoarding cloth or electronics is not makruh. Under Abu Yusuf, if such hoarding harms the public, it is makruh. Muhammad's view would exempt cloth entirely.
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is not specified here, but later Hanafi works tend to follow Abu Yusuf or a compromise.
4
Connect to the dalil
The proof for the prohibition is the hadith: 'The importer is blessed, and the hoarder is cursed.' This hadith is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalalah, so the ruling is makruh rather than haram.
The curse indicates strong disapproval, but not necessarily absolute prohibition.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Consider a trader hoarding medical supplies during a pandemic. Under Abu Yusuf, this would be ihtikar because it harms the public; under Abu Hanifa, since medicine is not qut, it might not be ihtikar per se, but could still be prohibited under other principles.
This shows the need to consider broader principles when new cases arise.

First, regarding tashir (adding markers every ten verses) and naqt (dotting) of the Quran: it is not disliked except for non-Arabs who need it, for whom it is considered good (hasan). Second, regarding ihtikar (hoarding): hoarding the staple food (qut) of humans and animals is makruh when it harms the people of the city. The author presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the definition of hoarding. Abu Hanifa restricts the prohibition to staple food (qut). Abu Yusuf expands it to any commodity whose detention harms the public. Muhammad al-Shaybani adds that there is no hoarding in cloth (i.e., clothing is not subject to hoarding rules). The proof for the ruling on hoarding is the hadith: 'The importer is blessed, and the hoarder is cursed' (Ibn Majah). The underlying reason is that hoarding harms the public by creating scarcity.

Describing the Throne with 'Izz: Permissible vs. Makruh
Clarification on permissible expressions about the Throne

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissible and makruh expressions regarding the Throne and 'izz, and explain why the anthropomorphic interpretation is invalid.

Core Idea
Describing the Throne itself as 'izz is permissible; attributing Allah's 'izz to the Throne is makruh by consensus.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the author addresses a subtle theological-linguistic issue: how to correctly interpret the phrase 'bi ma'qid al-'izz min 'arshik'. The ruling hinges on whether 'izz is ascribed to the Throne or to Allah in a way that implies location. Understanding this distinction protects one from falling into anthropomorphism (tajsim).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
First interpretation: making 'izz an attribute of the Throne itself (i.e., the Throne is described with 'izz). Second interpretation: making 'izz an attribute of Allah and relating it to the Throne (i.e., Allah's 'izz is attached to the Throne).
The second interpretation implies location for Allah.
2
Determine the permissible interpretation
The first interpretation is permissible because the Quran itself describes the Throne with attributes of grandeur and nobility (majd, karam).
Quranic precedent allows this.
3
Identify the prohibited interpretation and its ruling
The second interpretation is makruh by the agreement of all imams. The passage explicitly says 'the opposite is makruh by agreement'.
Consensus (ittifaq) among the imams.
4
Explain the reason for prohibition
The prohibited expression implies 'istiqamah' (sitting/establishment) which implies location (makan) for Allah. This is the belief of the Mujassimah (anthropomorphists) and is false (bātil).
Avoiding tajsim is essential in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جعل عزاً صفة للعرش ؛ لأنّ العرش موصوف في القرآن بالمجد والكرم، وكذا بالعزّ، وأما العكس فمكروه بالاتفاق، لاستقامته، من القعود المنبئ عن المكان ؛ لأن المراد من القعود هو التمكن على العرش، وذلك قول المجسمة، وهو باطل (۳).
ينظر: «ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٨٥). (٤) في «ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٨٦). (٥) في سنن ابن ماجة (۲) : (۷۲۸)، قال الكناني في «المصباح (۳) : ۲ (۱۰ : إسناده ضعيف، وله شواهد في سنن الدرامي» (۲) : (٣٢٤) ، و(سنن البيهقي الكبير » (٦) : ٣٠)، و(مسند عبد بن حمید» (١) : ٤٢).
And if one makes 'izz an attribute of the Throne, because the Throne is described in the Quran with grandeur and generosity, and likewise with might, but the opposite is makruh by agreement, due to its implying sitting (istiqamah) which indicates location, because the intended meaning of sitting is being established on the Throne, and that is the saying of the Mujassimah (anthropomorphists), and it is false. See: Dhakhirat al-'Uqba; etc.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction regarding the phrase 'bi ma'qid al-'izz min 'arshik'. It states that if one means by 'izz' an attribute of the Throne itself (i.e., the Throne is described with might), then it is permissible, because the Quran describes the Throne as majestic and noble. However, the opposite—attributing 'izz to Allah and implying that it is located on or attached to the Throne—is makruh by consensus. This is because it suggests that Allah 'sits' or is confined to a place, which is the belief of the Mujassimah (those who liken Allah to creation) and is false (bātil). The passage also references that the phrase 'al-qa'ud' (sitting) in this context implies physical location, which is rejected. The text then provides citations to Dhakhirat al-'Uqba and other works, including a hadith on hoarding with a weak chain.

Key Terms
مجسمة (Mujassimah): Those who attribute a physical body or location to Allah, including the belief that He sits on the Throne.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on saying that Allah's 'izz is attached to the Throne?
A) Permissible
B) Makruh by agreement
C) Haram
D) Wajib

Key Takeaway
Describing the Throne with 'izz is permissible, but attributing Allah's 'izz to the Throne in a way that implies location is makruh by consensus, as it leads to anthropomorphism.

Definition and Ruling on Reviving Dead Land (Ihya al-Mawat)
Defining dead land and explaining the legal ruling on reviving it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define dead land (al-mawat) and state the Hanafi ruling on reviving it, citing the primary evidence from the hadith.

Core Idea
Reviving dead land grants ownership — a permission rooted in the hadith 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.'

This passage opens the chapter on reviving dead lands (Ihya al-Mawat). It defines what constitutes dead land, provides the textual proof from the Sunnah, and clarifies related terminology. Understanding this foundation is essential before exploring the detailed conditions and disputes in later sections.

Guided Path
1
1. Define dead land
The passage defines dead land as land without benefit due to lack of water or salinity. Identify the key characteristics.
The definition is physical, not legal.
2
2. Locate the hadith
The commentator cites the hadith 'من أحيا أرضاً ميتة فهي له' as the legislative basis.
This hadith is narrated in multiple collections.
3
3. Understand the legal effect
The hadith explicitly states ownership for the reviver. The commentary confirms that the reviver owns what he revives.
No permission from ruler is required in Hanafi school.
4
4. Clarify technical terms
The passage explains sabkha (salty land) and adiyya (ancient waste). Understand their precise meanings.
Adiyya is a figurative expression.
5
5. Synthesize the ruling
Combine the definition, evidence, and effect to state the ruling: reviving a dead land makes the reviver the owner.
This is the foundation of the chapter.

كتاب إحياء الموات
(هي أرض بلا نفع لانقطاع مائها أو غلبته عليها أو نحوهما)، كما إذا نزَّت أو صارت سبخة (١٣) ، (عادية )
[1] قوله : كتاب إحياء الموات؛ مناسبة هذا الكتاب بكتاب الكراهة يجوز أن يكون من حيث أنّ مسائل هذا الكتاب ما يكره وما لا يكره، وأما مشروعية إحياء الموات فبقوله : «من أحيا أرضاً ميتة فهي لـه ) ، وإحياء الأرض عبارة عن جعلها بحيث ينتفع به، وسببه تعلق البقاء المقدور، وحكمه تملك المحيي ما أحياه. كذا في «العناية»(۳). [٢]قوله : إحياء الموات ؛ هو حيوان مات، وإنّما سُمِّي بالترجيع به من الأرض مواتاً ؛ لبطلان الانتفاع بها، تشبيهاً بالحيوان إذا مات فيبطل الانتفاع بها، فالمراد بإحياء المواتِ تشبيه للحياة النامية، قال الله : فَأَحْيَيْنَا بِهِ الْأَرْضَ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهَا كَذَلِكَ ) ، وفي الشرع ما ذكره المصنّف بقوله : وهي الأرض لجلب يا الله
[٣] قوله : سبخة ؛ بفتح السين المهملة وكسر الباء الموحدة، والخاء المعجمة: أرض مالحة لا تنبت شيئا. قاله الجلبي " [٤]قوله : عادية ؛ ليس المراد به ما يقتضيه ظاهر لفظه من أن يكون منسوباً إلى عاد ؛ لأنه لم يملك جميع أراضي الموات، بل المراد أنها متقدّمة الخراب، كأنها قريب في عهد عاد، وفي العادات الظاهرة ما يوصفُ بطول مضي الزمان عليه ينسب إلى عاد،
Book of Reviving Dead Lands. (It is land without benefit due to its water being cut off or its being overcome [by water/salt] or the like), such as when it becomes saline or becomes a sabkha (salty land), (ancient waste). [1] His statement: Book of Reviving Dead Lands; the connection of this book to the book of Karahah may be from the aspect that the issues of this book concern what is disliked and what is not disliked. As for the legislation of reviving dead land, it is by his statement: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.' And reviving land is an expression of making it such that benefit is derived from it. Its cause is the attachment of subsistence that is within capability. And its ruling is that the reviver owns what he revived. This is from al-`Inayah. [2] His statement: Ihyā' al-Mawāt; it is a dead animal. It is only called by the term (mawāt) applied to land by way of returning to it because of the loss of benefit from it, analogizing it to an animal when it dies, its benefit ceases. So the intended meaning of Ihyā' al-Mawāt is a simile for the growing life. Allah said: 'Thus We revive the earth after its death.' And in the Shari'ah, it is what the author mentioned by his statement: 'And it is the land...' [3] His statement: sabkha; with fatha on the sin and kasra on the ba and the mu'jama kha: salty land that grows nothing. Al-Jalabi said it. [4] His statement: 'adiyya; what is meant is not that which its apparent wording requires of being attributed to 'Aad, because he did not own all the dead lands. Rather what is meant is that it is ancient in ruin, as if it is near to the era of 'Aad. And in apparent customs, something described by the long passage of time upon it is attributed to 'Aad.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on reviving dead lands (Ihya al-Mawat). It first defines dead land as land from which no benefit can be derived due to water shortage, salinity, or similar reasons. The commentator explains the relevance of placing this chapter after the one on disliked matters (Karahah). The proof for the ruling is the hadith: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is his.' Revival is defined as making the land useful, and the cause (sabab) for ownership is the effort of subsistence. The ruling (hukm) is that the reviver owns the land. The term 'mawat' originally means a dead animal, and it is applied to land by analogy because both lose their benefit. The commentator clarifies that 'sabkha' means salty, barren land, and 'adiyya' means ancient waste, not necessarily from the tribe of 'Aad, but a time so remote it is as if from the era of 'Aad.

Key Terms
إحياء الموات (Ihya al-Mawat): Reviving dead land by making it useful for cultivation or construction, leading to ownership.
سبخة (sabkha): Salty land that cannot grow plants.
عادية (adiyya): Ancient waste land, so old it is attributed to the tribe 'Aad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal effect of reviving a dead land according to the Hanafi school?
A) The land becomes public property.
B) The reviver becomes its owner.
C) The land belongs to the closest heir.
D) The ruler decides who owns it.

Key Takeaway
Reviving dead land by making it beneficial leads to ownership by the reviver, as established by the hadith 'من أحيا أرضاً ميتة فهي له'.

Definition and Proof of Reviving Dead Lands
Definition and proof for reviving dead land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of dead land and identify the hadith proof that grants ownership to the reviver.

Core Idea
Reviving a dead land makes it the property of the reviver, as established by the Prophet's hadith.

This lesson opens the chapter on reviving dead lands (Ihyā' al-Mawāt). The text first defines what constitutes 'dead land'—land with no benefit due to lack of water or salinity, and which has been desolate since ancient times. It then presents the primary proof: the Prophet's statement that whoever revives such land owns it. The commentary clarifies that 'adiah does not mean belonging to the tribe of 'Ad but rather 'ancient ruin.' Understanding this definition and proof is essential for grasping the Hanafi position on land ownership through revival.

Guided Path
1
Define dead land (al-mawāt)
Identify the characteristics: no benefit, loss of water or overwhelming salt, and ancient desolation (ādiyah). Note the simile to a dead animal.
The term 'adiyah is clarified as 'ancient ruin,' not a tribal attribution.
2
Cite the hadith proof
State the hadith: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is for him.' This is the direct dalīl for the ruling.
This hadith is the foundation of the chapter.
3
Understand the meaning of revival (ihyā')
Revival means making the land beneficial, e.g., by cultivating, building, or irrigating. The cause is the need for sustenance.
The ruling is ownership of what is revived.
4
Address the cross-madhhab dispute
Note that the Shāfi'ī school requires Imām's permission, while the Hanafī school considers the hadith sufficient without permission.
This is a legitimate ikhtilāf based on different usūl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب إحياء الموات هي أرض بلا نفع لانقطاع مائها أو غلبته عليها أو نحوهما عادية. والموات تشبيهاً بالحيوان الميت. وأما مشروعية إحياء الموات فبقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «من أحيا أرضاً ميتة فهي له». وإحياء الأرض عبارة عن جعلها بحيث ينتفع به. وسببه تعلق البقاء المقدور، وحكمه تملك المحيي ما أحياه. وقوله عادية: ليس المراد به ما يقتضيه ظاهر لفظه من أن يكون منسوباً إلى عاد؛ لأنه لم يملك جميع أراضي الموات، بل المراد أنها متقدمة الخراب، كأنها قريب في عهد عاد.
Book of Reviving Dead Lands. It is land without benefit due to cutoff of its water or its overwhelming [salt] or similar, [and it is] ancient [in desolation]. And 'dead' is a simile to a dead animal. As for the legality of reviving dead land, it is by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is for him.' And reviving the land is an expression of making it such that benefit is derived from it. Its cause is the need for sustenance that is possible, and its ruling is that the reviver owns what he revived. And his saying 'ancient': it does not mean what its apparent wording requires, that it is attributed to 'Ad, because he did not own all the dead lands, rather it means that it is of ancient ruin, as if it is recent in the era of 'Ad.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on reviving dead lands. It first defines dead land: land from which no benefit can be derived due to loss of water or salinity, and it has been desolate since ancient times. The author then cites the famous hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as the primary proof: 'Whoever revives a dead land, it is for him.' The commentary explains that 'dead' is a metaphor likening useless land to a dead animal. Reviving means making the land productive. The cause of the ruling is human need for land, and the ruling itself is ownership by the reviver. The term 'adiah is explained: it does not mean 'related to the tribe of 'Ad' but rather 'ancient' as a conventional expression for land that has been ruined for a long time.

Key Terms
إحياء الموات (ihyā' al-mawāt): Act of making barren, unusable land productive
الموات (al-mawāt): Dead land, land without benefit due to water loss or salinity
عادية (ādiyah): Land that has been in ruins since ancient times, metaphorically attributed to 'Ad

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence for the ruling that reviving dead land grants ownership?
A) Quranic verse
B) Hadith of the Prophet
C) Consensus of companions
D) Rational analogy

Key Takeaway
Reviving a dead land—making it productive—grants the reviver ownership, based on the Prophet's hadith, with no need for the Imam's permission in the Hanafi school.

Conditions for Reviving Dead Land: Distance and Imam's Permission
Explains conditions of distance, unknown ownership, and Imam's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for reviving dead land in the Hanafi school and explain the intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab differences regarding distance and permission.

Core Idea
Dead land revival requires Imam's permission and sufficient distance; land once owned is never dead.

In this passage, the author explains the legal framework for reviving dead land (ihya' al-mawat). Two key conditions emerge: the land must be far enough from inhabited areas that no voice carries, and the revival must be authorized by the Imam. We also encounter a split between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the distance condition, and a broader dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i on whether a dhimmi can own revived land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of land
Distinguish between land previously owned in Islam (with unknown owner) and truly dead land that has never been owned. Muhammad considers the former not dead; it belongs to Muslims collectively.
This is a key nuance: not all abandoned land is mawat.
2
Apply the distance criterion
Use the voice test: a man with a loud voice stands at the edge of the inhabited area and shouts; if the sound is not heard at the land in question, it is sufficiently distant.
Abu Yusuf makes this a condition; Muhammad does not for previously owned land.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf requires distance from the inhabited area for land to be considered dead. Muhammad al-Shaybani does not require distance for land that was previously owned in Islam, as it is not dead land regardless of location.
This affects whether the land can be revived at all.
4
Recognize the role of the Imam's permission
The revival of dead land only grants ownership if the Imam (ruler) has given explicit permission. Without it, the land remains state property even if physically revived.
This applies to both Muslims and dhimmis.
5
Compare with Shafi'i position on non-Muslim ownership
Hanafis allow a dhimmi to own revived land by analogy (qiyas) to Muslims owning non-Muslim property through conquest. The Shafi'i school rejects this, holding that non-Muslims cannot own Muslim lands.
The practical consequence: dhimmi ownership of revived land is valid in Hanafi but invalid in Shafi'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو مملوكة في الإسلام لا يعرف مالكها بعيدة عن العامر لا يسمع صوت من أقصاها. وعند محمد ما كان مملوكاً لمسلم أو ذمي لا يكون مواتاً، فإذا لم يعرف مالكها كان لعامة المسلمين، ولو ظهر مالكها تُرَدُّ إليه ويضمن نقصان الأرض. والبعد عن العامر شرطه أبو يوسف خلافاً لمحمد. ومن أحيا ملكه إن أذن له الإمام ولو ذمياً وإلا فلا. وإن كان يسمع فلا لأنه فناء العامر. وبيان البعد: يقوم رجل جهوري الصوت من أقصى العمرانات على مكان عال وينادي بأعلى صوته، فأي الموضع الذي لا يسمع صوته فيه يكون بعيداً. وأما الشافعي فلا يقول بتملك الكافر أموالنا ولو استولى علينا.
Or [land] owned in Islam whose owner is unknown, far from inhabited area, the sound from its farthest end is not heard. According to Muhammad, what was owned by a Muslim or a dhimmi is not dead land; if its owner is unknown, it belongs to the general Muslims, and if its owner appears, it is returned to him and he is guaranteed the loss of the land. Distance from the inhabited area is a condition according to Abu Yusuf contrary to Muhammad. And whoever revives, he owns it if the Imam gives permission, even if he is a dhimmi; otherwise not. If [the voice] is heard, then no, because it is the outskirts of the inhabited area. And the explanation of distance: a man with a loud voice stands at the farthest part of the inhabited area on a high place and shouts at the top of his voice; the place where his voice is not heard is far. As for al-Shafi'i, he does not say that a disbeliever can own our wealth even if he takes control.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage details conditions for reviving dead land. First, it mentions a second category: land previously owned in Islam but with unknown owner. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, such land is not dead; it belongs to Muslims collectively. If the owner appears, it is returned with compensation. Abu Yusuf requires distance from inhabited area, while Muhammad disagrees. The practical criterion for distance: a loud voice from the farthest building must not be heard. Additionally, revival requires the Imam's permission. Even a dhimmi can own revived land with permission. Without permission, revival does not grant ownership. The passage concludes with a comparison to Imam al-Shafi'i, who does not permit non-Muslims to own Muslim lands through conquest, whereas Hanafi allows it by reciprocity.

Key Terms
العامر (al-'āmir): Inhabited area, village or town
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission from the Imam or ruler to revive dead land
الذمي (al-dhimmī): Non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what is a condition for land to be considered dead and subject to revival?
A) It must be owned by a Muslim
B) It must be far from inhabited area so that no voice is heard
C) It must have been owned by a dhimmi
D) It must have been abandoned for 100 years

Key Takeaway
Revival of dead land is not a private acquisition; it is regulated by the Imam and requires that the land be truly unowned and distant.

Reviving Dead Land: Conditions and the Dhimmi Dispute
Ruling on reviving remote dead land with Imam's permission and dhimmi capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on reviving dead land with Imam's permission, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on remoteness and permission, and evaluate the Shafi'i objection regarding dhimmi ownership.

Core Idea
Revival grants ownership only with Imam's permission; this applies equally to Muslims and dhimmis, contrary to Shafi'i.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses two interrelated issues: the conditions for valid revival of dead land, and the capacity of a dhimmi to acquire ownership through revival. The text navigates intra-Hanafi differences between Imam Muhammad, Abu Yusuf, and Abu Hanifa, and then defends the Hanafi position against the Shafi'i school regarding dhimmi ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The text discusses: (1) land once owned but now unknown owner, (2) remote dead land, and (3) revival with and without Imam's permission. Note how each scenario leads to a different ruling.
The land with unknown owner is not dead land; it belongs to the community.
2
Understand intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf requires the land to be remote (shout not heard) as a condition for revival; Muhammad does not. Similarly, Abu Hanifa requires Imam's permission; the Sahibayn do not. Recognize these as valid differences within the school.
The text presents Abu Hanifa's view as the default, but the mufta bihi often follows Sahibayn.
3
Grasp the key ruling
The central ruling: 'Whoever revives land owns it if the Imam permits him, otherwise not.' Without permission, the reviver does not gain ownership regardless of remoteness.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Analyze the dhimmi analogy
The Hanafi school extends the same ruling to dhimmis by analogy with conquest: just as Muslims can own unbelievers' property by conquest, unbelievers can own Muslim property by conquest or revival. This is the Hanafi principle (asluna).
The analogy is qiyas: the illa (cause) is that ihya' is a cause of ownership like istila'.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a dhimmi cannot own Muslim property even by conquest, so by extension, revival does not grant ownership to a dhimmi. The Hanafi response reaffirms the symmetry of ownership causes.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that in Shafi'i law, a dhimmi cannot acquire dead land by revival even with permission; in Hanafi law, he can.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو مملوكة في الإسلام لا يعرفُ مالكها بعيدةً عن العامر لا يسمع صوت من أقصاها : وعند محمد له ما كان مملوكاً لمسلم أو ذمي لا يكون مواتاً ، فإذا لم يعرف مالكها كان لعامة المسلمين، ولو ظهر مالكها تُرَدُّ إليه ويضمن نقصان الأرض، والبعد عن العامر شرطه أبو يوسف خلافاً لمحمد لها. (ومن أحيا مَلَكَهُ إن أذن له الإمامُ ولو ذميّاً "" وإلا فلا ) : أي إن لم يأذن فمعناه ما تقدَّم خرابه. قاله جلبي. قوله : ولو ذمياً ؛ لأنَّ الإحياء سبب الملك، إلا أن عند أبي حنيفة له إذن الإمام من شرطه، فيستويان فيه كما في سائر أسباب الملك، حتى الاستيلاء على أصلنا، يعني إنا نملك مال الكافر بالاستيلاء ، فكذا الكافر يملك مال المسلم بالاستيلاء ، فكذا هاهنا ، وأما الشافعي الله فلا يقولُ بتملك الكافر أموالنا ولو استولى علينا.
Or land owned in Islam whose owner is unknown, remote from inhabited area, where the voice of one at its farthest end is not heard. According to Muhammad (r.a.): that which was owned by a Muslim or a dhimmi is not dead land. So if its owner is unknown, it belongs to the general Muslim community; if its owner appears, it is returned to him and the deficiency of the land is guaranteed. The condition of remoteness from inhabited area is stipulated by Abu Yusuf contrary to Muhammad (r.a.). (Whoever revives land, owns it if the Imam permits him, even if he is a dhimmi, otherwise not): i.e., if he does not permit, the meaning is as previously (it remains) abandoned. Said by Chalabi. His statement: even if a dhimmi; because reviving is a cause of ownership, except that according to Abu Hanifa (r.a.), the Imam's permission is a condition for it; so they are equal in it as in other causes of ownership, even by conquest according to our principle: meaning we own the property of the unbeliever by conquest, so likewise the unbeliever owns the property of the Muslim by conquest; so likewise here. As for al-Shafi'i (r.a.), he does not say that an unbeliever owns our property even if he conquers us.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on reviving dead lands (ihya' al-mawat). It addresses two issues: (1) land that was owned in Islam but whose owner is unknown, and (2) the revival of remote dead land with the Imam's permission. The text first clarifies that land formerly owned by a Muslim or dhimmi does not become dead land even if abandoned; if the owner is unknown, it belongs to the Muslim community as a trust, and if the original owner appears, the land is returned but the reviver must pay compensation for any deficiency. On remoteness, Abu Yusuf requires the land to be far enough from the inhabited area that a loud shout from the edge is not heard, while Muhammad does not stipulate this. Then the text states that revival grants ownership only with the Imam's permission, and the permission applies equally to Muslims and dhimmis. The commentator explains that revival is a cause of ownership, but Abu Hanifa requires the Imam's permission as a condition. The equality of Muslim and dhimmi in this is analogized to other causes of ownership, such as conquest where both parties can own each other's property. The Shafi'i school disagrees, holding that a dhimmi cannot own Muslim property even by conquest.

Key Terms
إحياء الموات (ihya' al-mawat): The act of reviving dead (uncultivated, ownerless) land to bring it into productive use, a cause of ownership in Hanafi law.
العامر (al-'amir): The inhabited area (town or village); land within the vicinity of which is considered attached to it and cannot be revived without permission.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who is protected under a covenant (dhimma).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He still owns it because remoteness alone suffices.
B) He does not own it; the land remains as it was (abandoned).
C) He owns it only if he is a Muslim.
D) The land becomes joint property between him and the Imam.

Key Takeaway
Revival of dead land grants ownership only with the Imam's permission, and this permission is equally effective for Muslims and dhimmis, a position upheld against Shafi'i opposition.

Reviving Land from Which Water Has Diverted
FIQH RULING: Conditions for reviving land where water has diverted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on reviving land from which water has diverted and identify the two conditions that determine its permissibility.

Core Idea
Land with possible returning water is not dead; revival is only allowed when the water cannot return.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a specific scenario in the law of reviving dead land (ihya al-mawat). When land was once irrigated but the water source has diverted, its legal status depends on whether the water can possibly return. The commentator clarifies the underlying condition: the land must be under the disposal of the Imam. We also consider the protected zone of inhabited areas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is reviving land from which water has diverted. The ruling is that it is not permissible if water can return; permissible if it cannot return.
2
Locate the condition
The condition for permissibility is that the land becomes under the Imam's control, which happens when water cannot return.
3
Note the exception
Even if water cannot return, revival is still not allowed if the land is within the harim of an inhabited area.
4
Understand the rationale
The land is not truly dead if water can return because it retains a connection to the water source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يجز إحياء ما عدل عنه الماء وجاز عوده ، فإن لم يجز جاز. أي إن لم يجز عود الماء جاز إحياؤه. لأنَّ شرط جواز الإحياء أن تكون الأرض تحت تصرف الإمام ، فإذا عدل عنه الماء ولم يكن عوده، وذهب عليه الماء فصار في تصرف الإمام، فيجوز إحياؤه إذا لم يكن حريماً لعامر. قاله جلبي
And it is not permissible to revive land from which water has been diverted and its return is possible; if its return is not possible, it is permissible. That is: if return of water is not possible, its revival is permissible. Because the condition for permissible revival is that the land be under the control of the Imam; so if water has diverted from it and its return is not possible, and the water has gone away from it, it becomes under the control of the Imam, so its revival is permissible if it is not part of the protected zone (harim) of an inhabited area. Said by Chalabi.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on reviving land that has been irrigated but the water source has diverted. The key condition is whether the water can return. If it can return (e.g., a river that may shift back), the land is not considered dead because it is still potentially attached to the water source; hence revival is not permitted. However, if the water has completely left and there is no possibility of return, the land becomes like dead land under the Imam's control, and revival is permissible provided it is not within the protected zone (harim) of an inhabited area. The commentator (Chalabi) explains the underlying rationale: the land must be under the Imam's disposal, and the departure of water makes it so.

Key Terms
عدل عنه الماء (adala 'anhu al-ma'): Water has diverted away from the land, either permanently or temporarily.
حريم العامر (harim al-'amir): The protected zone around an inhabited area, which is considered attached to it and cannot be revived by others.
إحياء (ihya): Reviving dead land by cultivating or developing it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for revival of land from which water has diverted?
A) The land must be within the harim of an inhabited area.
B) The water must have been diverted permanently with no possibility of return.
C) The Imam must give permission.
D) The land must have been previously owned.

Key Takeaway
Revival of land from which water has diverted is permissible only if the water cannot return and the land is not within the protected zone of an inhabited area.

Ruling on Marking Land (Tahjir) and the Three-Year Cultivation Requirement
Ruling on marking land and forfeiture for non-cultivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal effect of tahjir and identify the condition under which the priority right is forfeited.

Core Idea
Marking land does not confer ownership; only actual revival (cultivation) does.

The concept of tahjir (marking land with stones) is a preliminary step in reviving dead land, but it has limited legal effect. This passage from the Hanafi tradition clarifies that tahjir merely reserves a priority right to cultivate, and if the marker fails to do so within three years, the Imam may assign the land to someone else. The underlying rationale is the benefit of the Muslim community through tax revenue ('ushr and kharaj).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
Tahjir is the act of marking land to indicate intent to revive it. Determine whether it grants ownership or just a priority right.
The text clearly states that tahjir is not revival (ihya') and does not give ownership.
2
Note the condition and consequence
If the marker does not cultivate within three years, the Imam can give the land to another. This is the central ruling.
The three-year period starts from the marking, not from any later action.
3
Understand the rationale
The original grant of land was conditioned on cultivation to generate tax revenue for Muslims. If cultivation is not achieved, the purpose fails, and reassignment fulfills the objective.
This is a rational (dalil aqli) argument based on public interest.
4
Distinguish tahjir from ihya'
According to Muhammad, breaking clods and watering the land constitutes revival (ihya'), which does grant ownership. Mere marking does not.
This distinction is crucial: tahjir is only preparatory.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a case, decide whether the marker has a valid claim or loses it based on the three-year rule.
Consider the time elapsed and whether any cultivation occurred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن حَجَرَ أَرضاً ولم يعمرها ثلاث حجج دفعها الإمام إلى غيره. التحجير في الأصل وضعُ الأحجار ؛ ليعلم الناسُ أَنَّه أخذَها ، ثمَّ سمّى : الإعلام الذي لا يكون بوضع الأحجار ، وقيل : اشتقاقه من الحجر بالسكون، فإن كربها وسقاها فهو إحياء عند محمد الله. قوله : دفعها الإمامُ إلى غيره....الخ ; لأنَّ الدفع إلى الأوّل كان ليعمرها، فتحصل المنفعة للمسلمين من حيث العشر والخراج، فإذا لم يحصل يدفعه إلى غيره تحصيلاً للمقصود ; ولأنَّ التحجير ليس بإحياء ليملكه به ; لأنَّ الإحياء إنما هو العمارة.
Whoever marks (hajara) a land and does not cultivate it for three years, the Imam gives it to another. Tahjir originally means placing stones so that people know he has taken it; then it was called: the notification that is not by placing stones, and it is said: its derivation is from al-hajr (with sukun on jim). If he breaks the clods and waters it, it is revival (ihya') according to Muhammad (r.a.). His statement: the Imam gives it to another etc.; because the giving to the first was to cultivate it, so that benefit reaches Muslims in terms of 'ushr and kharaj; so if it is not achieved, he gives it to another to achieve the purpose; and because tahjir is not revival to own by it; because revival is only cultivation.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of tahjir (marking land) and its limited legal effect. Tahjir is the act of marking land with stones or similar signs to indicate that one intends to revive it. However, it does not confer ownership; it only gives a priority right. If the person who marked the land fails to actually cultivate it within three years, the Imam (government) can take it back and assign it to someone else who will cultivate it. The commentator explains the rationale: the original grant was conditioned on cultivation to generate tax revenue ('ushr and kharaj) for the Muslim community. If the holder does not fulfill this, the land reverts. Furthermore, Muhammad (the student of Abu Hanifa) holds that actually breaking clods and watering the land constitutes revival (ihya'), which would grant ownership, but mere marking does not.

Key Terms
التحجير (al-tahjir): The act of marking land (e.g., with stones) to indicate intent to revive it; a preliminary step that does not constitute full revival.
إحياء (ihya'): Actual cultivation or improvement of dead land, such as breaking clods and watering, which grants ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a person marks land but does not cultivate it for three years?
A) He loses any right and the Imam can give it to someone else.
B) He owns the land because marking is equivalent to revival.
C) The land remains his but he must pay a fine.
D) The three-year period starts from the time the Imam grants permission.

Key Takeaway
Marking land (tahjir) gives a three-year priority right but does not confer ownership; actual cultivation (ihya') is required to own the land.

Reviving Land with Diverted Water
Ruling on reviving dead land when water may return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which land from which water has been diverted can be revived, and explain the role of the Imam's disposal.

Core Idea
Dead land with possible water return is not mawat — revival requires the land to be under the Imam's disposal.

Reviving dead land (iḥyā' al-mawāt) is a means of acquiring ownership. However, not all abandoned land is considered dead. When water has been diverted from land but may return, the land is not truly dead and cannot be revived. This lesson explains the precise condition that makes such land eligible for revival, focusing on the role of the Imam's authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act in question is reviving land (iḥyā'). The ruling is that it is permitted only under specific conditions.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
Separate the case where water may return (revival not permitted) from where it cannot (revival permitted). The key is the likelihood of return.
The text says: 'if the return of the water is not possible, then it is permitted'.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The condition for valid revival is that the land must be under the Imam's disposal. Land that still has the potential to become fertile through natural water return is not yet under the Imam's authority.
Water returning would make the land arable without human effort, so it remains in its original state.
4
Apply the harim exception
Even if the land is under the Imam's disposal, it cannot be revived if it lies within the harim (protected precinct) of already cultivated land.
The harim is a buffer zone to prevent harm to existing cultivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يجز إحياء ما عدل عنه الماء وجاز عوده ، فإن لم يجز جاز. قوله: أي إن لم يجز عود الماء جاز إحياؤه؛ لأن شرط جواز الإحياء أن تكون الأرض تحت تصرف الإمام، فإذا عدل عنه الماء ولم يكن عوده، وذهب عليه الماء فصار في تصرف الإمام، فيجوز إحياؤه إذا لم يكن حريماً لعامر. قاله جلبي.
And it is not permitted to revive land from which water has been diverted and its return is possible; if it is not possible (to return), then it is permitted. His saying: meaning, if the return of the water is not possible, then reviving it is permitted; because the condition for permission of revival is that the land be under the Imam's disposal. So if water is diverted from it and there is no possibility of its return, and the water is gone, then it falls under the Imam's disposal, so reviving it is permitted if it is not the precinct of a cultivated area. Said by Jalbī.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on reviving dead land (ihya' al-mawat) where water has been diverted from it but may possibly return. The matn establishes that such land cannot be revived because it is not truly dead as long as water may return and make it livable without human effort. However, if the return is impossible, it is considered mawat and can be revived. The commentary clarifies the underlying principle: the condition for valid revival is that the land must be under the disposal of the Imam (the ruler). If water is diverted but may return, the land is still considered as originally possessed by its water source, not yet under the Imam. But if the water is completely lost, the land falls under the Imam's authority and can be given for revival, provided it is not within the harim (protected area) of already cultivated land. This is a subtle distinction that prevents disputes over land that may naturally become arable.

Key Terms
إحياء الموات (iḥyā' al-mawāt): Reviving dead land by cultivating it, which grants ownership under Islamic law.
عاد الماء (ʻāda al-māʼ): The possibility of water returning to the land, which prevents it from being considered mawat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is it permitted to revive dead land from which water has been diverted?
A) When the water is still present but not flowing
B) When the return of the water is impossible
C) When the land is within the harim of a cultivated area
D) When the water has been diverted by the owner

Key Takeaway
Reviving land is only valid when water cannot return and the land is under the Imam's authority.
