Shurb and Shafah: Defining Water Rights in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines shurb and shafah and states basic water rights from public sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shurb and shafah and apply the Hanafi ruling that uncontained water is common property with permissible use conditions.

Core Idea
Uncontained water is common property; each has the right to drink (shafah) and irrigate (shurb) within limits.

This passage opens the chapter on water rights by defining two key terms—shurb and shafah—and establishing the fundamental principle that all water not captured in a vessel is common to all. From this baseline, the text then details the conditions under which one may draw water for irrigation, animal care, or even dig canals, always balancing individual benefit with public harm. Understanding these definitions and their legal implications is the foundation for the more specific rulings that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key terms
Read the first sentence: 'al-shurbu: nasibu al-ma, wa al-shafatu: sharbu bani adama...' Note that shurb is the share of water (for irrigation), while shafah is specifically the drinking right for humans and animals.
The distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
2
State the general principle
After defining, the text says: 'wa kulli haqqiha fi kulli ma' lam yuhraz bi-ina'. This means every water not contained has a right for everyone. This establishes common ownership.
Uncontained water includes rivers, springs, seas, etc.
3
Apply the condition for public use
The text permits digging a canal from a public river 'in lam yadur bil-'ammah' (if it does not harm the public). Identify this condition as key to permissibility.
Harm to the public prohibits the act.
4
Relate to the Quranic proof
The verse from Surah al-Shu'ara (26:155) is cited: 'laha shurbun wa lakum shurbun yawmin ma'lum'. Connect this to the concept of designated water rights.
The verse is about Prophet Salih's she-camel, but it is used as a proof for allocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشرب] الشربُ : نصيب الماء، والشَّفَةُ شرب بني آدمَ والبهائم، ولكلِّ حقها في كلِّ ماء لم يحرز بإناء، وسقي أرضه من البحر ونهر عظيم كدجلة ونحوها ، وشق نهر لأرضه منها أو لنصب الرحى إن لم يضر بالعامة، وإن ضرّ فلا. لا سقي دوابه إن خيف تخريب النهر لكثرتها وأرضه وشجرة من نهر غيره وقناته وبئره إلا بإذنه، وله سقي شجر أو خضر في داره حملاً بجراره في الأصح. وكري نهر لم يُمْلَك من بيت المال ، فإن لم يكن فيه شيء فعلى العامة، وكري نهرٍ مُلِك على أهله من أعلاه لا على أهلِ الشَّفَةِ، ومَن جاوزَ من أرضه قد برئ. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. اعلم أن هذا الخلاف في النهر الخاص. قال الله تعالى: لها شرب ولكم شرب يوم معلوم.
Chapter on Drinking: Shurb is the share of water, and shafah is the drinking of humans and animals. Each has a right in every water that is not contained in a vessel. He may irrigate his land from the sea, a great river like the Tigris and the like, and dig a canal for his land from it or for setting up a watermill if it does not harm the public; if it harms, then no. He may not water his beasts if destruction of the river is feared due to their multitude, nor his land or a tree from another's river, qanat, or well except with his permission. He may water a tree or greens in his house by carrying it in jars in the most correct view. Digging a canal that is not owned from the public treasury is upon the public treasury; if there is nothing in it, then upon the public. Digging a canal that is owned is upon its owners from its top, not upon the people of shafah. Whoever passes from his land is absolved. This is according to Abu Hanifah. Know that this difference is regarding the private canal. Allah says: "For her a drink and for you a drink on a known day."
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage defines key terms and establishes basic water rights in Hanafi fiqh. 'Shurb' is the share of water for irrigation, while 'shafah' is the right to drink for humans and animals. All uncontained water (e.g., rivers, springs) is common property—anyone may use it, but not to the point of hoarding. From large public rivers (like the Tigris) or the sea, one may divert water via canals for irrigation or to power a mill, provided it does not harm the wider community. If harm is caused, it is prohibited. Watering one's land or trees from another's private river, qanat, or well requires the owner's permission, but watering trees or vegetables within one's own home is allowed even if the water is carried in jars—this is the relied-upon view (in al-ashahh). The cost of digging a public canal falls on the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal); if insufficient, the community must bear it. For a privately owned canal, only the owners (ahl al-shurb, those with irrigation rights) bear the cost, not those with only drinking rights (ahl al-shafah). Once a partner's land ends, he is no longer liable for further maintenance. This ruling is attributed to Abu Hanifah, and the fatwa is according to his position. The text also references the Quranic verse about Prophet Salih's she-camel (26:155) to affirm the concept of water rights.

Key Terms
الشرب (al-shurb): The share of water for irrigation; the right to water one's land or trees.
الشفة (al-shafah): The right of humans and animals to drink water; derived from 'shafah' meaning lip.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, from which water sources may one irrigate without permission?
A) Any river, including private ones
B) The sea and great public rivers like the Tigris
C) Any water contained in a vessel
D) Only from one's own well

Key Takeaway
Uncontained water is common property; use is mubah with the condition of no public harm.

Watering Private Property: Rulings on Canals and Carrying Water
Focuses on the use of private water sources and the cost of maintaining canals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that watering one's trees at home with carried water does not require the owner's permission, and distinguish the cost allocation for public vs private canals.

Core Idea
Carrying water into your home is permitted; the owner cannot stop you. For canals, only landowners share the cost, not those with only drinking rights.

After establishing the general right to use public water, the passage turns to the specific case of using water from a private source. It addresses three key scenarios: using another's private water (requires permission), carrying water into one's own home (permitted), and the obligation to maintain canals shared by landowners. The text also records an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the carrying water scenario and resolves it by choosing the more lenient view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for private sources
Read: 'la saqiya dawabbahu... min nahr ghayrihi wa qanatihi wa bi'rihi illa bi-idhnihi'. This states that watering one's land or trees from another's private river, qanat, or well requires the owner's permission.
This applies to water that is contained or owned.
2
Analyze the exception for carrying water
Then: 'wa lahu saqya shajar aw khadr fi darihi hamlan bijirarihi fi al-ashahh.' This is the relied-upon view that allows carrying water in jars to water trees/greens inside his own house without permission.
The word 'fi al-ashahh' indicates there is a dispute.
3
Understand the dispute
The madhhab dispute map explains that some later Hanafi scholars (Balkh) opposed this, but the majority (Abu Hanifah, al-Sarakhsi) permit it based on custom and ease.
The opposing view considered it analogous to watering outside property which requires permission.
4
Examine canal cost allocation
The passage distinguishes between public and private canals. For a canal not owned (public), digging cost is from Bayt al-Mal or community. For a privately owned canal, cost is on the owners (ahl al-shurb) from its top, not on ahl al-shafah.
Once a partner's land ends, he is no longer liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشرب] الشربُ : نصيب الماء، والشَّفَةُ شرب بني آدمَ والبهائم، ولكلِّ حقها في كلِّ ماء لم يحرز بإناء، وسقي أرضه من البحر ونهر عظيم كدجلة ونحوها ، وشق نهر لأرضه منها أو لنصب الرحى إن لم يضر بالعامة، وإن ضرّ فلا. لا سقي دوابه إن خيف تخريب النهر لكثرتها وأرضه وشجرة من نهر غيره وقناته وبئره إلا بإذنه، وله سقي شجر أو خضر في داره حملاً بجراره في الأصح. وكري نهر لم يُمْلَك من بيت المال ، فإن لم يكن فيه شيء فعلى العامة، وكري نهرٍ مُلِك على أهله من أعلاه لا على أهلِ الشَّفَةِ، ومَن جاوزَ من أرضه قد برئ. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. اعلم أن هذا الخلاف في النهر الخاص. قال الله تعالى: لها شرب ولكم شرب يوم معلوم.

For a privately owned canal, only the owners (ahl al-shurb, those with irrigation rights) bear the cost, not those with only drinking rights (ahl al-shafah). Once a partner's land ends, he is no longer liable for further maintenance. This ruling is attributed to Abu Hanifah, and the fatwa is according to his position. The text also references the Quranic verse about Prophet Salih's she-camel (26:155) to affirm the concept of water rights.

Key Terms
الشرب (al-shurb): The share of water for irrigation; the right to water one's land or trees.
الشفة (al-shafah): The right of humans and animals to drink water; derived from 'shafah' meaning lip.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who bears the cost of digging a privately owned canal?
A) The public treasury (Bayt al-Mal)
B) All users including those with only drinking rights
C) The owners (ahl al-shurb) from the top, not those with only drinking rights (ahl al-shafah)
D) The owner of the land at the lowest point

Key Takeaway
Private water use requires permission, but carrying water into one's home is an exception; canal costs are only on those with irrigation rights.

Water Rights Without Land and Division of Shared Water
Rulings on water rights and division among partners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on claiming water rights without land and apply the proportional division rule for shared water among partners.

Core Idea
Water rights can be owned independently of land by istihsan, and shared water is divided proportional to land, not equally.

This passage from the chapter on reviving dead lands addresses two practical issues: whether a person can claim a water right (sharb) without owning the land, and how to divide a shared water source among multiple claimants. The Hanafi school applies istihsan to allow the claim, overriding the strict qiyas that would reject it. For division, the water is apportioned based on land area, not equally by heads, reflecting the benefit each partner derives. A sub-issue on dredging costs reveals a difference within the school between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First, the passage states that claiming a water right without land is valid (sahih). Second, if a group disputes over a shared water right, it is divided according to the measure of their lands.
These are two distinct but related rulings.
2
Examine qiyas versus istihsan for the first ruling
The qiyas (analogy) would deem the claim invalid because water cannot be owned without land, similar to wine. But istihsan overrides this, allowing the claim because water rights can be inherited or retained after selling the land.
Understand the reasoning: istihsan is based on custom and necessity.
3
Understand the proportional division rationale
The water is divided according to land area because the benefit of irrigation is proportional. An objection that possession of water is equal among partners is rebutted: benefit differs, so possession through use is not equal.
Compare with shared road division by heads, where ownership is unknown.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on dredging costs
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that dredging costs are shared equally from start to end of the channel. Imam Abu Hanifa holds distribution based on benefit (proportional to land and location).
This is a standard difference within the school.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The fatwa is given according to Imam Abu Hanifa's view, as noted in the margin reference to Rad al-Muhtar. Thus, dredging costs are shared based on benefit.
The relied-upon position is that of the Imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح دعوى الشرب بلا أرض فإن اختصم قوم في شرب بينهم قسم بقدرِ أراضيهم وقالا : عليهم كريه من أ ن أوله إلى آخره . (وصح دعوى الشرب بلا أرض) ، هذا استحسان ؛ لأنه قد يملك بدون الأرض إرثا ، وقد يباع الأرض ويبقى الشرب للبائع. (فإن اختصم قوم في شرب بينهم قسم بقدر أراضيهم ) قوله : هذا استحسان....الخ ؛ والقياس أن لا يصح؛ لأن المدعي يطلب من القاضي أن يقضي له بالملك فيما يدعيه إذا ثبت دعواه بالبينة، والشرب لا يحتمل التمليك بغير أرض، فلا يسمع القاضي فيه الدعوى، كالخمر في حق المسلمين، وأيضاً لأن شرط صحة الدعوى إعلام المدعي كالشهادة والشرب مجهول جهالة لا تقبل الإعلام ودليل الاستحسان ما قاله الشارح بقوله : لأنه ...الخ. [٢] قوله : قسم بقدر أراضيهم.... الخ ؛ لأن المقصود الانتفاع بسقيها، فيتقدر بقدره. فإن قيل : إنهم قد استووا في إثبات اليد على الماء الذي في النهر، والمساواة في اليد توجب المساواة في الاستحقاق. قلنا : إن إثبات اليد على الماء إنما هو بالانتفاع بالماء، ومـن لـه عشرة قطاع لا يكون انتفاعه مثل انتفاع من له قطعة واحدة، فلا يتحقق التساوي في إثبات اليد. بخلاف الطريق المشترك بين الجماعة، ولا يعرف كيف أصله بينهم، اختلفوا في ذلك، فإنه يقسم بينهم على عدد الرؤوس لا على قدر أملاكهم، حتى يعطى لصاحب القليل ما يعطى لصاحب الكثير، وفي الشرب يعطى لصاحب الكثير أكثر مما يعطى لصاحب القليل
And the claim of water right without land is valid. And if a group disputes over a water right among them, it is divided according to the measure of their lands. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Upon them is the dredging from the beginning to the end. (And the claim of water right without land is valid), this is istihsan; because it may be owned without land through inheritance, and the land may be sold while the water right remains for the seller. (If a group disputes over a water right among them, it is divided according to the measure of their lands.) His statement: This is istihsan... etc.; and the qiyas is that it is not valid; because the claimant asks the judge to rule for him ownership of what he claims if his claim is proven by evidence, and water right does not admit ownership without land, so the judge does not hear the claim concerning it, like wine regarding Muslims; also because the condition for validity of a claim is that the claimant informs (the opponent) like a testimony, and water right is unknown in a way that does not admit information. And the evidence of istihsan is what the commentator said: because... etc. [2] His statement: Divided according to measure of their lands... etc.; because the purpose is benefit by irrigating them, so it is proportioned to its measure. If it is said that they are equal in establishing possession of the water in the river, and equality in possession necessitates equality in entitlement, we say: Establishing possession of water is only through benefit from water, and whoever has ten plots does not have benefit like one who has a single plot, so equality in establishing possession is not realized. Unlike a shared road among a group, and the original ownership among them is unknown, they differed about that; for it is divided among them by number of heads, not according to their properties, so that the owner of little is given what the owner of much is given, while in water right the owner of much is given more than the owner of little.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage begins with two interrelated rulings from the chapter on reviving dead lands (Ihya al-Mawat). The first ruling states that it is valid for a person to claim ownership of a water right (sharb) without accompanying land. This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference), which overrides the strict qiyas (analogy) that would deem such a claim invalid. The reason istihsan is applied is that water rights can be owned independently, for example, through inheritance or when the land is sold but the water right is retained by the seller. The qiyas would reject the claim because water per se cannot be owned without land, similar to wine for Muslims. Additionally, a claim requires that the subject be known to the adversary, and a water right is considered unknown. The second ruling concerns the division of water among multiple claimants. The water is divided in proportion to the area of their respective lands, because the benefit of irrigation is proportional to the area. An objection is raised that all partners have equal physical possession of the water in the river, so they should share equally. The response is that possession of water is realized through actual use, and those with larger land use more water, so possession is not equal. This is contrasted with a shared road, where ownership is unknown and the road is divided equally by heads, regardless of adjacent property size. The text also mentions a sub-ruling regarding the cost of dredging the river: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the cost is shared from the beginning to the end equally. However, the fatwa is according to Imam Abu Hanifa, as noted in the footnote, where dredging costs are distributed based on benefit—the upstream partners bear less as the water passes their land, and the downstream partners bear more.

Key Terms
الشرب (al-sharb): The right to water for irrigation, considered a separate property right that can be owned independently of land.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that abandons an apparent analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable ruling, often based on custom, necessity, or a stronger evidence.
الكري (al-kuray): The cost of dredging or cleaning a water channel to maintain water flow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is shared water divided among partners in a water right?
A) Equally by number of partners
B) Proportionally to the area of their lands
C) According to the length of river frontage
D) By prior user rights

Key Takeaway
Water rights can be claimed independently of land by istihsan, and shared water is divided proportionally to land area, with dredging costs allocated by benefit following the Imam's view.

Restrictions on Upstream Damming and Constructions on Shared Rivers
Ruling on prohibiting upstream damming and unpermitted constructions on a shared river.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on damming a shared river and installing structures without partner consent, and identify the condition that permits such actions.

Core Idea
No partner may alter a shared river without consent—damming, digging, or building requires agreement.

Imagine you share a river with neighbors. Can you build a dam or a waterwheel? Islamic law protects the rights of all partners. This lesson from the Hanafi school explains the restrictions and exceptions, based on the principle of avoiding harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited actions
List the specific actions that are prohibited on a shared river without partner consent: damming (sakr), digging a canal (shaqq nahr), installing a mill (raha), waterwheel (daliyah), or bridge (jisr).
2
Understand the reason for prohibition
Each prohibition is based on harm: damming stops water flow to downstream partners; digging a canal breaks the shared riverbank; installing structures occupies shared space or alters water course.
The commentary specifies harm to the river (breaking the bank) and harm to the water (changing its course).
3
Note the exception
All prohibitions are lifted with the consent of all partners, as their rights are voluntarily waived. Consent can be explicit or through an agreement on scheduled use.
The text says 'except with their consent' (illa bi-ridahum).
4
Recognize the case of minimal harm
Installations are allowed if they cause no harm to the river or water, or if built on the partner's own land, because it is disposition in their own property and does not affect shared rights.
An exception is also noted for the 'owner of the little' (sahib al-qalil), but not elaborated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١(١٣) ومنع الأعلى منهم من سكر النهر وإن لم يشرب بدونه إلا برضاهم، وكل منهم من شق نهر منه ، ونصب رحى أو دالية أو جسر عليه بلا أذن شريكه، إلا لصاحب القليل، وهذا إذا لم يعلم مقدار حقهم ، وإذا علم يقسم على ما كان في الأصل. [١] قوله : ومنع الأعلى...الخ ؛ يعني إن كان الأعلى منهم لا يشرب حتى يسكر النهر ، فإن سكر منع من السكر لما فيه من إبطال حق الباقين، ولكنه يشرب بحصته. [٢] قوله : وإن لم يشرب ؛ أي لا يمكنه أن يسقي أرضه بتمامها إلا بالسكر، فينساق كل الماء إليه، ليس له ذلك إلا أن يكون صاحب الأعلى من بقعة لا يصل الماء إليها، إلا أن يتَّخذ في النهر سكر أو أرباب الأرضين مقرون بأنّ مشربها من هذا النهر، فلا بد أن يسكر النهر من أعلاه حتى يرتفع الماء إليها، والسكر: حبس الماء من الجريان. [٣]قوله : إلا برضاهم ؛ يعني تراضوا على أن يسكر الأعلى النهر حتى يشرب بحصته أو اصطلحوا على أن يسكر كل رجل منهم في نوبته جاز ؛ لأنّ المنع كان حقهم وقد زال بتراضيهم. [٤] قوله : وكل منهم.... الخ ؛ أي منع كلّ منهم من شق نهر منه، سواء كان يكري من النهر الأرض كان شربها من هذا النهر أو لا ؛ لأنّ فيه كسر ضفة النهر، وهي مشتركة بينه وبين وغيره. كذا في «الهداية». [0]قوله : ونصب رحى.... الخ ؛ أي منع نصب....الخ ؛ لأنّ فيه شغل موضع مشترك بالبناء إلا أن يكون نصب هذه الأشياء لا يضر بالنهر أو بالماء، أو يكون موضعها في أرض صاحبها ؛ لأنه تصرّف في ملك نفسه، ولا ضرر في حق غيره، ومعنى الضرر بالنهر ما بيناه من كسر الضفة، وبالماء أن يتغيَّرَ عن سَنَنه الذي كان يجري عليه.
13. And the upstream one among them is prevented from damming the river even if he cannot irrigate without it, except with their consent. And each of them is prevented from digging a canal from it, and installing a mill or a waterwheel or a bridge on it without the permission of his partner, except for the owner of the little (land). And this is if the measure of their right is not known; and if known, it is divided according to what was originally. [1] His statement: And the upstream one is prevented...etc.; meaning if the upstream one cannot irrigate except by damming the river, then if he dams, he is prevented from damming because it invalidates the right of the others, but he irrigates with his share. [2] His statement: Even if he cannot irrigate; i.e., he cannot water his land completely except by damming, so all the water flows to him, he does not have that right unless the upstream owner is in a place where water does not reach him, unless a dam is made in the river or the landowners have agreed that their water source is this river, so it is necessary to dam the river from its beginning so that the water rises to it. And as-sakr is stopping water from flowing. [3] His statement: Except with their consent; i.e., if they mutually agree that the upstream one dams the river so that he irrigates with his share, or they agree that each man dams in his turn, it is permitted; because the prohibition was their right, and it has ceased with their mutual consent. [4] His statement: And each of them...etc.; i.e., each one is prevented from digging a canal from it, whether he is dredging the river for the land or not; because it involves breaking the bank of the river, and it is shared between him and others. This is in al-Hidayah. [0] His statement: And installing a mill...etc.; i.e., prevented from installing...etc.; because it involves occupying a shared place with construction, unless the installation of these things does not harm the river or the water, or their place is in his own land; because it is a disposition in his own property, and there is no harm to the right of others. And the meaning of harm to the river is what we explained about breaking the bank, and to the water is that it changes from its course that it was flowing on.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya addresses the rights and restrictions of partners in a shared water channel. The main rulings are: (1) The upstream partner is prohibited from damming the river, even if he cannot irrigate without it, except with the consent of all partners. The reason is that damming harms the rights of others by preventing water from flowing downstream. If all partners consent, it is permissible. (2) Each partner is prohibited from digging a new canal from the river or installing a mill, waterwheel, or bridge without the permission of the other partners. This applies even if the partner owns land that draws water from the river, because digging damages the riverbank, which is common property. However, if the construction does not harm the river or the water, or if it is built on the partner's own land, it is permissible. The commentary elaborates that 'harm to the river' means breaking the bank, and 'harm to the water' means altering its natural flow. An exception is made for the owner of a very small plot (صاحب القليل), but the text does not elaborate further. Finally, if the exact shares of the partners are unknown, the division is based on the original state; if known, it is divided accordingly.

Key Terms
السكر (as-sakr): A dam or barrier to stop the flow of water in a river.
شق النهر (shaqq an-nahr): Digging a new canal or channel from the main river.
دالية (daliyah): A waterwheel or device for raising water from the river.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is it permissible for an upstream partner to dam a shared river?
A) Whenever his land needs water
B) With the consent of all partners
C) Only if he owns more than half the land
D) It is never permissible

Key Takeaway
In a shared river, no partner may unilaterally alter the watercourse or install structures without the consent of all partners, to prevent harm to shared rights.

Damming a Shared River: Upper Riparian Consent
Ruling on damming a shared river with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an upper partner may not dam a shared river without unanimous consent, and identify when such damming becomes permissible.

Core Idea
You cannot dam a shared river even to irrigate your own land without every partner's consent.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on damming (sakr) a shared river. Even if the upper riparian owner cannot irrigate without damming, he is prohibited from doing so unless all partners agree. The goal is to protect the water rights of all shareholders.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is damming a shared river by an upper partner. The default ruling is prohibition; the text says 'the upper is prevented from damming'.
Prohibition applies even if he cannot drink without it.
2
Recognize the condition for permissibility
The prohibition lifts only with the consent of all partners (bi-ridahum). If they agree, the upper may dam according to his share or in turns.
Consent must be unanimous; majority is insufficient.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Damming nullifies the rights of downstream partners by impounding water. Their right to the water is joint property; thus unilateral harm is not allowed.
The underlying principle: protection of shared rights overrides individual need.
4
Consider the exception for necessity
If the upper land cannot receive water without a dam, and the partners have established that the water source is this river, then the upper must dam – but only with the partners' consent or a pre-existing arrangement.
This is not a permission to act unilaterally; it reinforces the need for agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنع الأعلى منهم من سكر النهر وإن لم يشرب بدونه إلا برضاهم، وكل منهم من شق نهر منه، ونصب رحى أو دالية أو جسر عليه بلا أذن شريكه، إلا لصاحب القليل، وهذا إذا لم يعلم مقدار حقهم، وإذا علم يقسم على ما كان في الأصل. [١] قوله : ومنع الأعلى...الخ ؛ يعني إن كان الأعلى منهم لا يشرب حتى يسكر النهر ، فإن سكر منع من السكر لما فيه من إبطال حق الباقين، ولكنه يشرب بحصته. [٢] قوله : وإن لم يشرب ؛ أي لا يمكنه أن يسقي أرضه بتمامها إلا بالسكر، فينساق كل الماء إليه، ليس له ذلك إلا أن يكون صاحب الأعلى من بقعة لا يصل الماء إليها، إلا أن يتَّخذ في النهر سكر أو أرباب الأرضين مقرون بأنّ مشربها من هذا النهر، فلا بد أن يسكر النهر من أعلاه حتى يرتفع الماء إليها، والسكر: حبس الماء من الجريان. [٣]قوله : إلا برضاهم ؛ يعني تراضوا على أن يسكر الأعلى النهر حتى يشرب بحصته أو اصطلحوا على أن يسكر كل رجل منهم في نوبته جاز ؛ لأنّ المنع كان حقهم وقد زال بتراضيهم. [٤] قوله : وكل منهم.... الخ ؛ أي منع كلّ منهم من شق نهر منه، سواء كان يكري من النهر الأرض كان شربها من هذا النهر أو لا ؛ لأنّ فيه كسر ضفة النهر، وهي مشتركة بينه وبين وغيره. كذا في «الهداية». [0]قوله : ونصب رحى.... الخ ؛ أي منع نصب....الخ ؛ لأنّ فيه شغل موضع مشترك بالبناء إلا أن يكون نصب هذه الأشياء لا يضر بالنهر ولا بالماء، أو يكون موضعها في أرض صاحبها ؛ لأنه تصرّف في ملك نفسه، ولا ضرر في حق غيره، ومعنى الضرر بالنهر ما بيناه من كسر الضفة، وبالماء أن يتغيَّرَ عن سَنَنه الذي كان يجري عليه.
رحى وضع في ملكه ولا يضر بالنهر ولا بالماء، ومن توسيع فم النهر، ومن القسمة بالأيام وقد كانت بالكوى، ومن سوق شربه إلى أرض له أخرى ليس لها منه شرب، والشرب يورث ويوصى بالانتفاع. [١] قوله : من توسيع فم النهر الخ؛ أي منع من توسيع فم النهر...الخ ؛ لأنه يكسر ضفة النهر ويزيد على مقدار حقه في أخذ الماء، وكذا إذا أراد أن يؤخرها ؛ أي الكوى عن فم النهر ؛ لاختيار الماء فيه فيرد، أو دخول الماء. بخلاف ما إذا أراد أن يسفل كواه أو يرفعها، حيث يكون جاز له ذلك في الصحيح ؛ لأنَّ قسمة الماء في الأصل باعتبار سعة الكوة وضيقها من غير اعتبار التسفل والرفع، . العادة، هو فلم يكن فيه تغيير موضع القسمة. [٢] قوله : يستدل به على أنه حق تلك الأرض ؛ لأنهم يتنازعون في الشرب، فيقضي له بشرب الأرضين جميعاً إذا لم يعلم حقيقة الحال ؛ لأنّ الشرب على مقدار الأراضي إذا لم يعلم حقيقة الحال. «غاية البيان». [٣] قوله : والشرب يورث ؛ بناء على أنَّ الورثة خلف الميت، فيقومون مقامه في أملاكه وحقوقه، وعدم جواز بيعه وهبته وصدقته لا يستلزم عدم جواز ذلك. مع من القديم (۱) روزن: وهو الكَوَّة، فارسي معرب ينظر: «طلبة الطلبة»(ص١٣٦). (۲) بيانها ليس لواحد منهم أن يقسم بالأيام ولا مناصفة أن القسمة قد كانت بالكوى، وكذا لا يجوز أن يقسم بالكوى وقد كانت بالأيام ؛ لأن القديم يترك على قدمه إلا أن يرضى الكل. ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر» (٢ : ٥٦٦).
And the upper [partner] is prevented from damming the river even if he does not drink without it except with their consent. And each of them is prevented from digging a canal from it, and placing a mill or waterwheel or bridge upon it without the permission of his partner, except for the owner of little [water]? And this is when the amount of their right is not known, and when it is known it is divided according to what was in the origin. [1] His saying: 'and the upper is prevented...etc.': meaning if the upper among them does not drink unless he dams the river, then if he dams he is prevented from damming because it involves nullifying the right of the others, but he drinks according to his share. [2] His saying: 'and if he does not drink': i.e. he cannot irrigate his land entirely except by damming, so all the water flows to him, that is not permissible for him unless the upper owner is from a place that water does not reach, except that a dam is made in the river or the landowners agree that their drinking source is this river, then he must dam the river from its upper part so that water rises to it, and 'sakr' is impounding water from flowing. [3] His saying: 'except with their consent': meaning if they agree that the upper may dam the river so that he drinks according to his share, or they mutually agree that each man dams in his turn, it is permitted because the prohibition was their right and it has been removed by their mutual consent. [4] His saying: 'and each of them...etc.': meaning each of them is prevented from digging a canal from it, whether he rents the land from the river and its drinking is from this river or not, because it involves breaking the bank of the river, and it is shared between him and others. So in 'Al-Hidayah'. [0] His saying: 'and placing a mill...etc.': meaning prevention of placing...etc. because it involves occupying a shared place with construction, unless the placing of these things does not harm the river or the water, or their location is in the owner's land, because it is a disposition in his own property, and no harm in the right of others, and the meaning of harm to the river is what we explained of breaking the bank, and to the water is that it changes from its course which it used to flow. A mill placed in his property and does not harm the river or the water, and [prevention] from expanding the mouth of the river, and from division by days when it used to be by perforations (kawa), and from bringing his drinking to another land of his that does not have drinking from it, and the drinking right is inherited and bequeathed for benefit. [1] His saying: 'from expanding the mouth of the river...etc.': meaning prevention from expanding the mouth of the river...etc. because it breaks the bank of the river and increases beyond the measure of his right in taking water, and likewise if he wants to move them back, i.e. the kawa from the river mouth, to choose water in it so it returns, or the entry of water. Contrary to if he wants to lower his kawa or raise it, where it is permissible for him in the correct view, because the division of water originally is based on the width and narrowness of the kawa without consideration of lowering and raising; custom is it, so there is no change of the place of division. [2] His saying: 'it is deduced by it that it is the right of that land': because they dispute over drinking, so he is judged to have drinking for both lands if the reality of the situation is not known, because drinking is according to the measure of lands if the reality of the situation is not known. 'Ghayat al-Bayan'. [3] His saying: 'and the drinking right is inherited': based on that the heirs stand in place of the deceased, so they take his place in his properties and rights, and the impermissibility of selling it, gifting it, and giving it in charity does not entail the impermissibility of that. With from the old [custom?] (1) Rawzan: it is al-kuwwa, Persian arabicized, see 'Talbat al-Talaba' (p.136). (2) Its explanation: it is not for any one of them to divide by days nor half if the division used to be by kawa, and similarly it is not permissible to divide by kawa if it used to be by days, because the old [custom] is left in its old state unless all agree. See 'Majma' al-Anhur' (2:566).
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from the Hanafi work Al-Hidayah, covering a set of rulings on water rights among partners sharing a river or canal. The commentator explains each ruling in sequence. First, the upper riparian owner (al-a'la) is not permitted to dam the river (sakr) even if he cannot irrigate without doing so, unless all partners consent. Damming would cut off water from downstream partners, infringing their established right. However, the upper owner may take his share of water through other means. The term 'sakr' is defined as impounding water to stop its flow. Consent of all partners removes the prohibition because the right to object belongs to them. Secondly, each partner is prohibited from digging a separate canal from the main river, or installing a mill (raha), waterwheel (daliya), or bridge (jisr) without the permission of the others. The reason is that these actions involve breaking the riverbank (kharq al-daffa), which is shared property. Exceptions are made for the holder of a negligible share (sahib al-qalil) or when these structures are placed entirely on one's own land and cause no harm to the river's flow or bank integrity. Harm is defined as physical damage to the bank or alteration of the water's natural course. The text then addresses additional rules: expanding the mouth of the river is forbidden because it increases the taker's share and may damage the bank. Changing the position of irrigation holes (kuwa) horizontally is prohibited, but vertical adjustment (raising or lowering) is permissible in the correct view, as division is based on hole size, not height. If there is a dispute over whether a land has a water right, and the facts are unclear, the presumption is that the right is proportional to the land area. Finally, the right of water (al-shurb) is inheritable and can be bequeathed for usufruct, though it cannot be sold, gifted, or given in charity. A footnote clarifies the term 'kuwwa' and adds that if the customary division was by days or by holes, it must be maintained unless all partners agree to change.

Key Terms
السكر (al-sakr): Damming a river by impounding water to stop its flow; a structure that blocks water.
الشرب (al-shurb): The right to take water from a source for irrigation or drinking; a legal right attached to land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rulings in this passage, what is the ruling if an upper partner dams the river without the consent of the other partners?
A) Permissible because of his need to irrigate
B) Prohibited because it nullifies the rights of others
C) Permissible if the lower partners do not object
D) Permissible only if he is unable to irrigate otherwise

Key Takeaway
An upper partner may not dam a shared river without unanimous consent, even if he cannot irrigate otherwise, because it infringes on the joint water rights of all partners.

Prohibitions on Canals, Mills, and Bridges Without Consent
Commentary on prohibitions related to shared river structures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi prohibitions on digging canals, installing mills, waterwheels, or bridges on a shared river without partner permission, and identify the exceptions based on harm and ownership.

Core Idea
No one may alter a shared riverbank or structure without unanimous consent, unless it is on his own land and causes no harm.

This lesson covers the second set of rulings in the passage: each partner is prohibited from digging a separate canal, installing a mill, waterwheel, or bridge without permission of all partners. The rationale is that these actions affect the shared riverbank and water flow. However, exceptions exist when the structure is on one's own land and causes no harm, or for the holder of a negligible share.

Guided Path
1
List the prohibited acts
The text lists: digging a canal (shaqq nahr), installing a mill (raha), waterwheel (daliya), or bridge (jisr) on the river without permission.
These acts are mubah (permissible) only with unanimous consent.
2
Identify the reason for prohibition
These acts involve breaking the riverbank (kharq al-daffa), which is shared property. Harm is defined as physical damage to the bank or changing the water's course.
The riverbank is jointly owned; any alteration requires consent.
3
Recognize exceptions
Exceptions: the owner of a small share (sahib al-qalil) may be allowed if the harm is negligible, or if the structure is placed on one's own land and causes no harm to the river or water flow.
The key is whether harm occurs to shared property.
4
Apply the principle to specific cases
Expanding the river mouth is also prohibited because it increases the taker's share and may damage the bank. Similarly, moving irrigation holes (kuwa) horizontally is prohibited, but vertical adjustment (raising/lowering) is permissible in the correct view.
Custom (urf) plays a role in determining permissible adjustments.

Source Text

ومنع الأعلى منهم من سكر النهر وإن لم يشرب بدونه إلا برضاهم، وكل منهم من شق نهر منه، ونصب رحى أو دالية أو جسر عليه بلا أذن شريكه، إلا لصاحب القليل، وهذا إذا لم يعلم مقدار حقهم، وإذا علم يقسم على ما كان في الأصل. [١] قوله : ومنع الأعلى...الخ ؛ يعني إن كان الأعلى منهم لا يشرب حتى يسكر النهر ، فإن سكر منع من السكر لما فيه من إبطال حق الباقين، ولكنه يشرب بحصته. [٢] قوله : وإن لم يشرب ؛ أي لا يمكنه أن يسقي أرضه بتمامها إلا بالسكر، فينساق كل الماء إليه، ليس له ذلك إلا أن يكون صاحب الأعلى من بقعة لا يصل الماء إليها، إلا أن يتَّخذ في النهر سكر أو أرباب الأرضين مقرون بأنّ مشربها من هذا النهر، فلا بد أن يسكر النهر من أعلاه حتى يرتفع الماء إليها، والسكر: حبس الماء من الجريان. [٣]قوله : إلا برضاهم ؛ يعني تراضوا على أن يسكر الأعلى النهر حتى يشرب بحصته أو اصطلحوا على أن يسكر كل رجل منهم في نوبته جاز ؛ لأنّ المنع كان حقهم وقد زال بتراضيهم. [٤] قوله : وكل منهم.... الخ ؛ أي منع كلّ منهم من شق نهر منه، سواء كان يكري من النهر الأرض كان شربها من هذا النهر أو لا ؛ لأنّ فيه كسر ضفة النهر، وهي مشتركة بينه وبين وغيره. كذا في «الهداية». [0]قوله : ونصب رحى.... الخ ؛ أي منع نصب....الخ ؛ لأنّ فيه شغل موضع مشترك بالبناء إلا أن يكون نصب هذه الأشياء لا يضر بالنهر ولا بالماء، أو يكون موضعها في أرض صاحبها ؛ لأنه تصرّف في ملك نفسه، ولا ضرر في حق غيره، ومعنى الضرر بالنهر ما بيناه من كسر الضفة، وبالماء أن يتغيَّرَ عن سَنَنه الذي كان يجري عليه.
رحى وضع في ملكه ولا يضر بالنهر ولا بالماء، ومن توسيع فم النهر، ومن القسمة بالأيام وقد كانت بالكوى، ومن سوق شربه إلى أرض له أخرى ليس لها منه شرب، والشرب يورث ويوصى بالانتفاع. [١] قوله : من توسيع فم النهر الخ؛ أي منع من توسيع فم النهر...الخ ؛ لأنه يكسر ضفة النهر ويزيد على مقدار حقه في أخذ الماء، وكذا إذا أراد أن يؤخرها ؛ أي الكوى عن فم النهر ؛ لاختيار الماء فيه فيرد، أو دخول الماء. بخلاف ما إذا أراد أن يسفل كواه أو يرفعها، حيث يكون جاز له ذلك في الصحيح ؛ لأنَّ قسمة الماء في الأصل باعتبار سعة الكوة وضيقها من غير اعتبار التسفل والرفع، . العادة، هو فلم يكن فيه تغيير موضع القسمة. [٢] قوله : يستدل به على أنه حق تلك الأرض ؛ لأنهم يتنازعون في الشرب، فيقضي له بشرب الأرضين جميعاً إذا لم يعلم حقيقة الحال ؛ لأنّ الشرب على مقدار الأراضي إذا لم يعلم حقيقة الحال. «غاية البيان». [٣] قوله : والشرب يورث ؛ بناء على أنَّ الورثة خلف الميت، فيقومون مقامه في أملاكه وحقوقه، وعدم جواز بيعه وهبته وصدقته لا يستلزم عدم جواز ذلك. مع من القديم (۱) روزن: وهو الكَوَّة، فارسي معرب ينظر: «طلبة الطلبة»(ص١٣٦). Key Terms
الكوة (al-kuwwa (plural: al-kuwa)): A perforation or hole made in wood or a channel to let water flow to fields; a device for dividing water shares.

Water Division Methods: Kawa, Days, and Inheritability
Commentary on division methods and legal nature of water rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rules on dividing water shares by kawa or days, identify when vertical adjustment of kawa is permissible, and state that the right of water (shurb) is inheritable but not transferable through sale or gift.

Core Idea
Water division must follow established custom; vertical adjustment of water holes is permissible, but horizontal change is not, and the water right inherits but cannot be sold.

This lesson focuses on the concluding portions of the passage, which deal with methods of dividing water shares (by perforations/kawa or by days), adjustments to irrigation holes, and the legal nature of the water right (shurb). The Hanafi position emphasizes that established customs are binding, vertical adjustments are permissible, and the right of water is inheritable but not alienable.

Guided Path
1
Understand division by custom
If the water share was originally divided by kawa (perforations in a channel), it cannot be changed to division by days without unanimous consent, and vice versa. The old method is preserved ('the old is left on its old state') unless all agree to change.
Custom (urf) becomes a binding legal method.
2
Distinguish vertical vs horizontal adjustment
Moving irrigation holes (kuwa) horizontally (forward/backward) is prohibited because it changes the place of division. However, raising or lowering the kawa vertically is permissible in the correct view, as division is based on hole size, not elevation.
The text says: 'because the division of water originally is based on the width and narrowness of the kawa without consideration of lowering and raising'.
3
Determine water right in disputes
If there is a dispute over whether a land has a water right, and the actual arrangement is unknown, the presumption is that the right is proportional to the land area ('drinking is according to the measure of lands').
This is a rule of presumption (asl) to resolve uncertainty.
4
Recognize the legal nature of shurb
The water right (shurb) is inheritable and can be bequeathed for usufruct (tawsiyat bi al-intifa). It cannot be sold, gifted, or given in charity because it is a servitude right attached to land.
The text states: 'the drinking right is inherited and bequeathed for benefit'.

Source Text

And this is when the amount of their right is not known, and when it is known it is divided according to what was in the origin. [1] His saying: 'and the upper is prevented...etc.': meaning if the upper among them does not drink unless he dams the river, then if he dams he is prevented from damming because it involves nullifying the right of the others, but he drinks according to his share. [2] His saying: 'and if he does not drink': i.e. he cannot irrigate his land entirely except by damming, so all the water flows to him, that is not permissible for him unless the upper owner is from a place that water does not reach, except that a dam is made in the river or the landowners agree that their drinking source is this river, then he must dam the river from its upper part so that water rises to it, and 'sakr' is impounding water from flowing. [3] His saying: 'except with their consent': meaning if they agree that the upper may dam the river so that he drinks according to his share, or they mutually agree that each man dams in his turn, it is permitted because the prohibition was their right and it has been removed by their mutual consent. [4] His saying: 'and each of them...etc.': meaning each of them is prevented from digging a canal from it, whether he rents the land from the river and its drinking is from this river or not, because it involves breaking the bank of the river, and it is shared between him and others. So in 'Al-Hidayah'. [0] His saying: 'and placing a mill...etc.': meaning prevention of placing...etc. because it involves occupying a shared place with construction, unless the placing of these things does not harm the river or the water, or their location is in the owner's land, because it is a disposition in his own property, and no harm in the right of others, and the meaning of harm to the river is what we explained of breaking the bank, and to the water is that it changes from its course which it used to flow. A mill placed in his property and does not harm the river or the water, and [prevention] from expanding the mouth of the river, and from division by days when it used to be by perforations (kawa), and from bringing his drinking to another land of his that does not have drinking from it, and the drinking right is inherited and bequeathed for benefit. [1] His saying: 'from expanding the mouth of the river...etc.': meaning prevention from expanding the mouth of the river...etc. because it breaks the bank of the river and increases beyond the measure of his right in taking water, and likewise if he wants to move them back, i.e. the kawa from the river mouth, to choose water in it so it returns, or the entry of water. Contrary to if he wants to lower his kawa or raise it, where it is permissible for him in the correct view, because the division of water originally is based on the width and narrowness of the kawa without consideration of lowering and raising; custom is it, so there is no change of the place of division. [2] His saying: 'it is deduced by it that it is the right of that land': because they dispute over drinking, so he is judged to have drinking for both lands if the reality of the situation is not known, because drinking is according to the measure of lands if the reality of the situation is not known. 'Ghayat al-Bayan'. [3] His saying: 'and the drinking right is inherited': based on that the heirs stand in place of the deceased, so they take his place in his properties and rights, and the impermissibility of selling it, gifting it, and giving it in charity does not entail the impermissibility of that. With from the old [custom?] (1) Rawzan: it is al-kuwwa, Persian arabicized, see 'Talbat al-Talaba' (p.136). (2) Its explanation: it is not for any one of them to divide by days nor half if the division used to be by kawa, and similarly it is not permissible to divide by kawa if it used to be by days, because the old [custom] is left in its old state unless all agree. See 'Majma' al-Anhur' (2:566).
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from the Hanafi work Al-Hidayah, covering a set of rulings on water rights among partners sharing a river or canal. The commentator explains each ruling in sequence. First, the upper riparian owner (al-a'la) is not permitted to dam the river (sakr) even if he cannot irrigate without doing so, unless all partners consent. Damming would cut off water from downstream partners, infringing their established right. However, the upper owner may take his share of water through other means. The term 'sakr' is defined as impounding water to stop its flow. Consent of all partners removes the prohibition because the right to object belongs to them. Secondly, each partner is prohibited from digging a separate canal from the main river, or installing a mill (raha), waterwheel (daliya), or bridge (jisr) without the permission of the others. The reason is that these actions involve breaking the riverbank (kharq al-daffa), which is shared property. Exceptions are made for the holder of a negligible share (sahib al-qalil) or when these structures are placed entirely on one's own land and cause no harm to the river's flow or bank integrity. Harm is defined as physical damage to the bank or alteration of the water's natural course. The text then addresses additional rules: expanding the mouth of the river is forbidden because it increases the taker's share and may damage the bank. Changing the position of irrigation holes (kuwa) horizontally is prohibited, but vertical adjustment (raising or lowering) is permissible in the correct view, as division is based on hole size, not height. If there is a dispute over whether a land has a water right, and the facts are unclear, the presumption is that the right is proportional to the land area. Finally, the right of water (al-shurb) is inheritable and can be bequeathed for usufruct, though it cannot be sold, gifted, or given in charity. A footnote clarifies the term 'kuwwa' and adds that if the customary division was by days or by holes, it must be maintained unless all partners agree to change.

Key Terms
الكوة (al-kuwwa (plural: al-kuwa)): A perforation or hole made in wood or a channel to let water flow to fields; a device for dividing water shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following changes to a kawa (irrigation hole) is permissible?
A) Moving it backward from the river mouth
B) Moving it forward toward the river
C) Lowering it vertically
D) Changing from kawa to days

Key Takeaway
Water division methods are binding by custom; vertical adjustment of irrigation holes is permissible, but water rights cannot be sold or gifted—only inherited or bequeathed for benefit.

Water Rights: Invalid Sales and Liability for Irrigation
States rulings on water transactions and neighbor liability with causal analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that water without land is not a valid subject of sale (batil) and explain the liability rule for irrigation damage, distinguishing between customary and excessive watering, and reconcile the positions of Khawahar Zadeh and al-Bazdawi.

Core Idea
Water separate from land is not valuable property; liability for irrigation damage turns on whether watering was customary.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses two distinct but related issues: the non-transferability of water rights apart from land, and the liability of a landowner when watering his land causes damage to a neighbor. The first ruling is absolute: water without land cannot be sold, given, or used in contracts because it is not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). The second ruling involves a dispute within the Hanafi school: is a landowner liable for seepage or flooding caused by normal irrigation? The commentary reconciles the varying positions by distinguishing between customary and excessive watering.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First, note that the passage establishes two distinct rulings: (1) water without land cannot be sold, rented, gifted, etc.; (2) a landowner who waters his land and causes damage to a neighbor may or may not be liable. Recognize these as separate legal issues.
The first is about the legal capacity of water as a commodity; the second is about tort liability.
2
Analyze the rationale for the ban on water transactions
The text gives three possible reasons: uncertainty (jahalah), risk (gharar), or water not being valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). Focus on the last: water separate from land lacks the quality of being a 'mal' (property) in Hanafi fiqh, thus cannot be exchanged in a sale or similar contract.
This rationale is supported by an analogy: certain things (like wine) can be inherited but not sold – the ability to own via inheritance does not imply saleability.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute on liability
The text presents the view of Khawahar Zadeh: no liability for normal watering. Then it cites al-Bazdawi from al-Jami' al-Saghir: liability regardless. Identify these two positions within the Hanafi school.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, not an inter-madhhab one.
4
Examine the causal reasoning (talil) for non-liability
The commentary explains that the landowner's action is a pure cause (sabab mahd) because the seepage is accidental. He does not transgress (ta'addi). The necessary effect of his action is water gathering in his own land, not the neighbor's seepage. Liability requires transgression, like digging a well or placing a stone in a public path.
The key distinction: sabab mahd vs. direct causation with transgression.
5
Apply the reconciliation: customary vs. excessive watering
The commentary harmonizes the two views: if watering is customary (i.e., the land normally absorbs it), no liability – following Khawahar Zadeh. If watering exceeds the land's capacity, the landowner is liable because he effectively directs water to the neighbor's land – vindicating al-Bazdawi's concern. This becomes the mufta bihi position.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is that in a lawsuit, the neighbor only gets compensation if the watering was excessive.

Source Text

حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية ولا يباع، ولا يؤجر، ولا يوهب ، ولا يتصدق به، ولا يجعل مهراً، وبدل الصلح. ولا يضمن من ملأ أرضه فنَزَّت أرضُ جاره، أو غرقت، ولا من سقى من شرب غيره، وهو قول الإمام المعروفِ بخواهر زاده ، وفي «الجامع الصغير» للبزدوي : أنه يضمن. ألا ترى أن القصاص والدين والخمر تملك بالإرث ، وإن لم تملك بالبيع ونحوه، والوصية أحب الميراث. (عناية). [١] قوله : ولا يباع ...الخ ؛ إما للجهالة أو للغرر أو لأنه ليس بمال متقوّم، حتى لا يضمن إذا سقى من شرب غيره. «هداية»(۲). [٢] قوله : وبدل الصلح ؛ أي لا يجعل الشرب بدل الصلح، صورته : إذا ادعى رجل قبل رجل دعوى في أرض أو دار فصالحه من دعواه علـى شــرب بغير أرض ، فالصلح باطل ؛ لأن الصلح إذا وقع على خلاف جنس الحقِّ كان فيه معنى البيع، وبيع الشرب بلا أرض لا يجوز ، فكذا لا يجوز الصلح على الشرب من غير أرض. «غاية البيان». 31) قوله : ولا يضمن...الخ ؛ لأنه غير متعد فيه؛ لأنّ كونَ الفعل علة للشيء إنما يعرف بالأثر اللازم له ، والأثر اللازم لفعله اجتماعُ الماء في أرضه، وإنما صارت أرض جاره ذات نز بالشرب والاجتذاب، وهو أمر اتفاقي قد يكون وقد لا يكون، فلا يضاف إلى فعله إلا أنه لولا فعله لما حصل هذا الفساد. فصار فعله في حق هذا الأثر سبباً محضاً، والمسبب إنما يضمن إذا تعدى كحافر البئر، وواضع الحجر، وفعله في أرضه مباح ، فلم يضمن قالوا : هذا إذا سقى أرضه سقياً معتاداً يتحمّل أرضه عادة، أما إذ سقى سقياً لا يتحمّل أرضه فيضمن ؛ لأنه أجرى الماء إلى أرض جاره تقديراً. «كفاية» (۳). (۱) (العناية» (۱۰ : ۸۷). (۲) «الهداية» (٤ : ١٠٧). (۳) «الكفاية» (۹: ۲۱).
Good guidance for the latter part of Sharh al-Wiqaya: And it (water) is not sold, nor rented, nor gifted, nor given as charity, nor made as dower, nor as substitute in a reconciliation. And one is not liable if he fills his land and his neighbor's land oozes or floods, nor if he waters from another's water source. This is the opinion of the well-known Imam Khawahar Zadeh. In al-Jami' al-Saghir by al-Bazdawi: that he is liable. Do you not see that retaliation (blood money), debt, and wine are owned through inheritance, even though they are not owned through sale and the like? And bequest is more beloved to inheritance. (al-Inayah). [1] His statement: 'and it is not sold...' either due to uncertainty (jahalah) or risk (gharar) or because it is not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), to the extent that if he waters from another's water source he is not liable. (Hidayah) [2] His statement: 'nor as substitute in reconciliation' means that water (shurb) is not made as a substitute for reconciliation. Its form: if a man claims something against another man concerning land or a house, and he reconciles with him regarding his claim for water without land, the reconciliation is invalid; because when reconciliation occurs contrary to the type of right, it contains the meaning of sale, and sale of water without land is not permissible, so likewise reconciliation for water without land is not permissible. (Ghayat al-Bayan) 31) His statement: 'and one is not liable...' because he is not transgressing in it; for knowing that an action is a cause for something is known by its necessary effect, and the necessary effect of his action is the gathering of water in his land. As for his neighbor's land becoming moist, that is due to the drawing and attraction of water, which is an accidental matter that may or may not occur, so it is not attributed to his action, except that without his action this corruption would not have occurred. So his action, with respect to this effect, became a pure cause (sabab mahd). The causer is only liable if he transgresses, like the digger of a well and the placer of a stone. His action in his land is permissible, so he is not liable. They said: this is if he waters his land with a customary watering that his land normally can bear. As for if he waters with a watering that his land cannot bear, then he is liable, because he effectively directed water to his neighbor's land. (Kifayah) (3) (al-Inayah 10:87), (Hidayah 4:107), (Kifayah 9:21).
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses two related rulings on water (shurb): transactions and liability. The author first states that water cannot be sold, rented, gifted, given as charity, made as dower, or used as a substitute in a reconciliation contract (sulh). The reason, explained in the commentary, is threefold: uncertainty (jahalah), risk (gharar), or because water without land is not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) in the Hanafi school. Since it is not mal, it cannot be the subject of a sale or similar contract. The second ruling concerns liability when a landowner irrigates his land and the water seeps into or floods his neighbor's land. The default position, attributed to Imam Khawahar Zadeh, is that he is not liable. However, Imam al-Bazdawi in his al-Jami' al-Saghir holds that he is liable. The commentary then harmonizes these views: if the watering is customary and the land can normally absorb it, there is no liability because the harm is accidental and the action is permissible. But if he overwaters beyond what his land can bear, he effectively directs water to the neighbor's land, making him liable. The proof for non-liability in the normal case is based on causation: the action is only a pure cause (sabab mahd), not a direct cause, because the seepage is an accidental effect. This is contrasted with the digger of a well or placer of a stone, where transgression is present. The passage also uses qiyas (analogy) with inheritance: certain things like wine, debt, and blood money can be owned through inheritance even though they cannot be sold, showing that the ruling is specific to the type of transaction.

Key Terms
شرب (shurb): Water rights or the water itself in the context of irrigation and land ownership; here it refers to water without associated land.
سبب محض (sabab mahd): Pure cause: an action that is the remote or indirect cause of an effect, not directly intended, and thus does not create liability unless accompanied by transgression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when is a landowner liable for damage caused by watering his land?
A) Always, because his action caused the damage.
B) Never, because watering is permissible.
C) Only if he waters in a way that exceeds his land's normal capacity.
D) Only if he waters from another's water source.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, water without land is not saleable, and a landowner is only liable for irrigation damage if he waters excessively beyond his land's normal capacity.

The Hanafi Definition of Khamr and the Eight Beverage Categories
Opens Kitab al-Ashriba with definition of khamr and eight beverage types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of khamr, identify its specific substance, and distinguish the eight beverage types with their rulings.

Core Idea
Khamr is only from grapes; other intoxicants are haram by qiyas, not by the same name.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, the commentator opens the chapter on beverages by providing a precise legal definition of khamr and then lists the eight major categories of drinks recognized in the Hanafi school. Understanding this definition is crucial because the term 'khamr' carries specific legal consequences, such as the hadd punishment and ritual impurity, which do not apply to other intoxicants. The passage also clarifies the usage of the term 'ashriba' in Shari'ah, restricting it to forbidden drinks. Let us walk through the definition and classification step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of khamr
Read the first part: 'Khamr is forbidden: it is that which from grape juice if it boils, becomes strong, and throws foam.' Note that khamr is specifically from grapes; other fruits are excluded.
Hanafi school strictly limits khamr to grape-derived drinks.
2
Understand the term 'ashriba' in legal context
The commentary explains that although 'sharab' means any drink linguistically, in Shari'ah usage it refers specifically to forbidden beverages. This is why the chapter is named 'Kitab al-Ashriba'.
This technical narrowing affects how we interpret rulings.
3
List the eight beverage types
The passage enumerates eight types: four forbidden (khamr, tila', naqi'an) and four permissible (muthallath, date-nabidh, khalitan, honey-nabidh). Note that the forbidden ones are haram even if not intoxicating.
The classification is from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq.
4
Distinguish between haram and halal categories
Identify the key difference: the forbidden beverages include those from grapes, raisins, and certain mixtures; the permissible ones involve specific preparation methods like muthallath (two-thirds reduced).
Later lessons will expand on each category.
5
Connect to broader usul
Observe that the definition sets the scope for the prohibition. The Hanafi position on khamr being only from grapes is based on the literal interpretation of the primary texts, while other schools expand it via qiyas.
This is a key point of ikhtilaf with the Shafi'i school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢]قوله : الأشربة ؛ وهي جمع شراب، وهو ما يشرب من المائعات، وأريد بها في هذا الكتاب ما حرم منها، وسمي هذا الكتاب بها؛ لأنّ فيه بيان أحكامها ، كما سمى كتاب الحدود لما فيه بيان أحكامها ، قيل : الشراب لغة : اسم لما يشرب سواء كان حلالاً أو حراماً ، أما في استعمال أهل الشرع ، فاسمُ الشراب يقوم على ما حَرُمَ منه. اعلم أن الأصول التي تتَّخذ منها الأشربة هذه الأشياء : العنب، والزبيب، والتمر، والفواكه والبرّ، والذرة والفرصاد، والدخن، والألبان، كلبن الإبل، والرماك، وغير ذلك. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية». (۱) تبيين الحقائق (٦ : ٤٥). (۲) «الهداية» (٤ : ١٠٨ - ١١٠).
The Book of Beverages: Khamr is forbidden: it is that which from grape juice if it boils, becomes strong, and throws foam. [1] His statement: 'The Book of Beverages'; he mentioned the Book of Beverages after (the chapter on) water (shurb) due to a connection between them in derivation, which is the sharing of the two words in the original meaning and root letters; but he placed water first because it is permissible, while beverages include forbidden ones like khamr. It is said: sharab linguistically is a name for what is drunk, whether halal or haram. As for the usage of the people of Shari'ah, the name sharab is used for what is forbidden from it. Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on beverages (Kitab al-Ashriba) in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. It first gives the definition of khamr: it is specifically from grape juice that boils, becomes strong, and throws foam. This is a key legal definition because in Hanafi fiqh, khamr is strictly from grapes; other intoxicating drinks are not called khamr but may still be haram by analogy. The commentary then explains the placement of this chapter after the chapter on water (shurb) due to linguistic connection and to contrast permissible (water) with forbidden (khamr). It also clarifies the term 'ashriba: in the Shari'ah usage, it refers specifically to forbidden drinks, even though linguistically it includes all drinks. The passage then lists the eight types of beverages recognized in the school: four that are haram even if not intoxicating (khamr, tila', and the two naqi'an drinks) and four that are halal (muthallath, date-nabidh, khalitan, and honey-nabidh). The commentary notes that this classification is from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq. Finally, it announces that the author will elaborate on ten topics from al-Hidayah concerning khamr, starting with its essence and the threshold for its name.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Specifically grape juice that has boiled, become strong, and thrown foam. In Hanafi, khamr is only from grapes; other intoxicants are not called khamr (though still haram).
أشربة (ashriba): Plural of sharab; in this book, it refers specifically to forbidden beverages, though linguistically it means any drink.

Key Takeaway
Khamr is a specific, technically defined term in Hanafi fiqh referring only to grape wine that has reached a certain stage, with distinct legal consequences.

Prohibition of All Intoxicating Beverages in Hanafi Fiqh
Establishes Hanafi position on prohibition of all intoxicating beverages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that all intoxicating beverages (including cooked grape juice and raw infusions) are haram li-aynihi, cite the dalil from hadith and ijma, and distinguish the Hanafi view from the Shafi'i, Awza'i, and Shuraik positions.

Core Idea
All intoxicants are khamr, and khamr is haram li-aynihi — cooking does not change the ruling.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya expands on the Hanafi ruling for intoxicating beverages, addressing a key dispute: are only certain intoxicants forbidden, or are all intoxicants inherently impure and prohibited? The Hanafi school holds the latter, based on the Quranic description of khamr as rijs and the consensus of the Companions. The passage also clarifies the definitions of tila', naqi' al-tamr, and naqi' al-zabib, and responds to opposing views from Shafi'i, Awza'i, and Shuraik.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the overall ruling: all intoxicating beverages (even if cooked) are haram li-aynihi and najis. The drinker faces hadd even without intoxication.
The Hanafi position does not limit khamr to grape wine.
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Note the positions of al-Shafi'i (cooking may permit), al-Awza'i (tila'/badhaq is permissible), and Shuraik (sukr/date wine is permissible from Quran 16:67).
Each opponent uses a different dalil.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Shuraik cites Quran 16:67 as permission. Awza'i relies on practice. Shafi'i argues that cooking changes the essence.
The Hanafi school acknowledges the verse but explains its meaning.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: Allah called khamr rijs (impurity) — this indicates inherent prohibition. The hadith 'Khamr comes from these two trees' (dates and grapes) and the consensus of the Companions establish that all intoxicants from these sources are khamr.
The verse about sukr is interpreted as referring to lawful provision, not intoxicant.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis prohibit all intoxicating drinks from grapes, dates, and raisins, even if cooked. Shafi'is allow some after cooking. Awza'i allows badhaq. Shuraik allows date wine.
This affects hadd penalties and everyday practice.

Then the one who declares it permissible becomes a disbeliever. Its value is nullified, not its monetary consideration. It is prohibited to benefit from it. The one who drinks it is punished with the hadd penalty even if not intoxicated. Cooking does not affect it (i.e., does not make it permissible). It is permissible to turn it into vinegar, contrary to al-Shafi'i. [3] These are ten rulings. (2) He said: its value is nullified... etc.; and it indicates that the one who destroys it and the usurper are not liable for compensation, and (4) the impermissibility of selling it, and Allah's ruling of it being rijs for degradation, because value (taqawwum) implies honor. If it is said: does the non-liability of the destroyer indicate permissibility of destroying it? We say: it does not indicate that, but there is a difference of opinion regarding it. Some say it is permissible to destroy it, others say it is not permissible except for a valid purpose, such as finding it with a sinful person and fearing his sinfulness. But if it is in the hand of a righteous person, it is not permissible to destroy it because he will make vinegar from it. This is the essence of what is in al-Hidayah and its commentaries. In al-Inayah: he only interpreted dates as wet dates because the product from dates is called nabidh al-tamr, except for al-sukr, and it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. So between the words of al-Jawhari and al-Hidayah there is some discrepancy, contemplate. Said al-Jalabi. He only came with this state in the dual form, while the state of the two nabidhs is in the singular form where he said cooked, although each is of two things, because the two naqi's are both mentioned, while of the two nabidhs only one is mentioned. Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute and ruling statement concerning the prohibition of intoxicating beverages. The commentator begins by explaining the matn which lists ten rulings about tila' (cooked grape juice). The first ruling that the commentator expands on is that tila' is najis (impure) and its prohibition is not limited to the intoxicating effect but applies to the substance itself. The Hanafi position is that all types of khamr (intoxicants) are haram li-aynihi (forbidden in essence), as Allah called them rijs (impurity). This is proven by the consensus of the Ummah. The passage then discusses that the one who declares it permissible becomes a disbeliever, the nullification of its value (taqawwum), the prohibition of benefiting from it, the hadd penalty for the drinker even if not intoxicated, that cooking does not make it permissible (contrary to Shafi'i), and that it is permissible to turn it into vinegar. The commentator then clarifies that the nullification of value is indicated by the fact that the destroyer or usurper is not liable for compensation, and the prohibition of selling. A question arises: does non-liability imply permissibility of destroying it? The answer is no; there is a difference of opinion on destroying it. The passage then addresses a discrepancy in the definition of tila': according to al-Muhit, tila' is what has lost two-thirds and becomes intoxicating, which is supported by the practice of senior Companions and the definition of al-Jawhari. On page 342, the text extends the ruling to naqi' al-tamr (date infusion) and naqi' al-zabib (raisin infusion) when raw and intoxicating. The pronoun 'they' refers to tila', date infusion, and raisin infusion. It then reports the positions of al-Awza'i (who allowed tila' as badhaq) and Shuraik ibn Abdullah (who allowed 'sukr'/date wine based on Quran 16:67). The Hanafi position rebuts these by citing the hadith 'Khamr comes from these two trees' (meaning from dates and grapes) and the consensus of the Companions. The commentator then delves into grammatical analysis: the term 'niyyayn' (raw/uncooked) is a state (hal) for the two naqi's, indicating their actual condition, not as a restriction, because the general understanding of naqi' is that it is uncooked, whereas nabidh is cooked. The dual form is used because both naqi's are explicitly mentioned, whereas nabidh often appears in the singular. Finally, the Hanafi proof is given: the hadith 'Khamr from these two trees' and the consensus of the Companions.

Key Terms
الطلاء (al-tila'): In this passage, tila' is defined as grape juice that has been cooked and less than two-thirds have evaporated, and it is considered najis. However, there is a discussion that the correct definition is that which has lost two-thirds and becomes intoxicating.
نقيع التمر / نقيع الزبيب (naqi' al-tamr / naqi' al-zabib): Infusions of dates and raisins, respectively, made without cooking (raw). When they become agitated and intoxicating, they are forbidden according to the Hanafis.
السكر (al-sukr): In this passage, al-sukr refers specifically to an intoxicating drink made from date water (rutab). It is discussed in relation to the permissibility according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but the overall ruling on intoxicants remains prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of cooking on the prohibition of an intoxicating beverage like tila'?
A) Cooking removes impurity and makes it permissible.
B) Cooking renders it permissible only if it no longer intoxicates.
C) Cooking does not affect the prohibition; it remains haram and najis.
D) Cooking makes it permissible to sell but not to drink.

Key Takeaway
All intoxicating beverages from grapes and dates are haram li-aynihi and najis according to the Hanafi school; cooking does not change this ruling.

intermediate
6 min
The Hanafi Ruling on Muthallath and the Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on muthallath permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on muthallath and explain the inter-madhhab dispute over its permissibility, citing the key proofs from both sides.

Core Idea
Muthallath is permissible in the Hanafi school because only khamr is prohibited by name; other intoxicants are not automatically haram.

This lesson addresses a classic fiqh dispute: the permissibility of muthallath, a grape juice concentrated by boiling. While the majority of schools deem it prohibited like any intoxicant, the Hanafi school permits it as long as it does not intoxicate. The disagreement hinges on textual interpretation: does the prohibition of khamr extend to all intoxicants, or is it specific? Let us examine the argument from both sides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Drink and Its Preparation
First, understand what muthallath is: grape juice boiled to one-third, then left to ferment. This technical definition is essential as it distinguishes it from khamr in the Hanafi argument.
Muthallath is not simply any fermented drink; it has a specific process.
2
State the Hanafi Position and Its Proof
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit muthallath. Their primary proof is the hadith: 'Khamr itself is prohibited, its small and large amount' (حرمت الخمر بعينها قليلها وكثيرها). They argue this limits the strict ruling to khamr only, not other drinks. Additionally, they note that muthallath is thick and nourishing, which does not lead to excess like khamr.
The Hanafi school distinguishes between khamr as a specific substance and other intoxicants.
3
Identify the Opposing Schools and Their Evidence
Imam Muhammad (within Hanafi), Imam Malik, and Imam al-Shafi'i hold muthallath is prohibited. They cite general hadiths: 'Every intoxicant is haram' (كل مسكر حرام) and 'Whatever intoxicates in large amount, its small amount is haram' (ما أسكر كثيره فقليله حرام). They also analogize it to khamr because it intoxicates.
The opposing view treats all intoxicants as equally prohibited under the umbrella of khamr.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response to the Opposing Proof
The Hanafis respond that the general hadiths are qualified by the specific hadith about khamr. Moreover, the Quranic term 'sukr' (intoxication) is interpreted by Hanafis as referring to nabidh, not muthallath. They also argue that the analogy (qiyas) fails because muthallath, due to its thickness, does not lead to intoxication in small amounts like khamr.
The Hanafi use of istihsan (juristic preference) allows them to set aside the qiyas.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The practical difference: a Hanafi may drink a non-intoxicating amount of muthallath, while followers of the other schools must avoid it entirely. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school is permissibility.
This is a real-life issue in communities where such drinks are prepared.
