And the solution of the triple-boiled grape juice (muthallath) is that it is prepared by boiling grape water until two-thirds evaporate and one-third remains, then it is left until it boils, becomes strong, and froths. Similarly, if water is poured into it after two-thirds have evaporated until it thins, then it is boiled slightly, then left to boil, become strong, and froth. The answer to the verse: It is abrogated according to the Hanafi and others, as stated in al-Kashshaf, or we say: 'sukr' refers to nabidh, which is juice of grapes, raisins, and dates when it remains for three days, permissible according to the Greatest Imam up to the limit of intoxication, by this verse. It is in al-Kashshaf. And the sukr mentioned in the verse is interpreted as such to reconcile the verse and hadith. It is said that the intended meaning is rebuke, not gratitude, meaning: in your foolishness you take from it a forbidden intoxicant and leave good provision. This was said by al-Jalabi. His words: 'And the solution of the triple-boiled grape juice... etc.' means as long as it does not reach the limit of intoxication, because the purpose is to explain the change of its ruling, which is the prohibition of drunkenness from it due to change in the meaning of khamr. It is specifically mentioned for grape muthallath because everything else becomes permissible with the slightest boiling, even if one-tenth does not evaporate, as is clear from his words: 'And nabidh of dates... etc.' as an apposition to muthallath. Al-Jalabi said. (A) His words: 'after its two-thirds have evaporated' because pouring water only increases it to double, unlike if poured before boiling, because water goes first due to its lightness, or goes from both, so the evaporated amount is not two-thirds of the grape water. (1) (2) (1) His words: 'And muthallath is only permissible according to Abu Hanifa...' because for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is his saying: 'Khamr itself is prohibited, its small and large amount' and 'the intoxicant from every drink' specifies that intoxication is prohibited in other than khamr, because the conjunction indicates differentiation; and because the corrupting element is the intoxicating cup, and it is forbidden according to us. It cannot be said: then the prohibited from khamr should only be the last cup. For we say: yes, analogy would be that, but we abandoned it because khamr, due to its lightness and subtlety, calls to much, so the small amount is given the ruling of the large amount, whereas muthallath is not like that because its thickness does not call to it, rather it is itself nourishment, so it remains on permissibility. And the evidence of the latter three is his saying: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden', and his saying: 'Whatever intoxicates in large amount, its small amount is forbidden', and his saying: 'Whatever intoxicates the jar, a gulp from it is forbidden'; and because the intoxicant corrupts the intellect, so it is forbidden in small and large amounts like khamr. And it is narrated from Muhammad that he holds like their view, and from him that he disliked it, and from him that he said: I do not forbid it nor permit it. And Allah knows best.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe commenting on the ruling of muthallath (triple-boiled grape juice). The author first describes the preparation of muthallath: grape juice is boiled until two-thirds evaporates, then left to ferment and froth. The same applies if water is added after evaporation. The commentary then addresses the interpretation of the Quranic verse about 'sukr' (intoxicant). The Hanafi position is that the verse was abrogated or that 'sukr' refers to nabidh, not muthallath, to reconcile with hadith. The core dispute is between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf who permit muthallath as long as it is not intoxicating, and Imam Muhammad, Imam Malik, and Imam al-Shafi'i who prohibit it based on the principle that any intoxicant is haram even in small amounts. The Hanafi argument distinguishes muthallath from khamr: khamr is light and leads to excess, so even a little is prohibited, while muthallath is thick and nourishing, so it remains permissible unless it intoxicates. The opponents rely on general hadiths like 'Every intoxicant is haram' and analogize it to khamr. The passage also reports multiple narrations from Imam Muhammad, some agreeing with the two shaykhs, some considering it disliked, and some refraining from a ruling. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that muthallath is permissible if not intoxicating.

Key Terms
مثلث عنبي (muthallath 'inabi): Grape juice boiled down to one-third of its original volume, then left to ferment and become strong.
سكر (sukr): Intoxication or the intoxicating drink; in the Quranic verse, interpreted differently by Hanafis.
نبيذ (nabidh): A drink made from dates, raisins, or grapes, often fermented.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary Hanafi evidence for permitting muthallath?
A) The hadith 'Every intoxicant is haram' is abrogated.
B) The hadith 'Khamr itself is prohibited, its small and large amount' limits the ruling to khamr only.
C) Muthallath is not mentioned in any hadith, so it is permissible.
D) The Quranic verse permits all drinks except khamr.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits muthallath as long as it does not intoxicate, because the prohibition of khamr is specific to that substance; other schools prohibit it based on general intoxicant prohibitions.

Permissibility of Date and Raisin Nabidh: The Two Conditions
Stating the ruling on nabidh with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for the permissibility of date and raisin nabidh and apply the Hanafi ruling when intention involves frivolity.

Core Idea
Nabidh is permissible if non-intoxicating and drunk without frivolous intent; even water becomes haram with such intent.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the ruling on nabidh made from dates and raisins. While the drink itself is permissible when boiled even slightly and not intoxicating, a key condition is the intention of the drinker. The author emphasizes that intending frivolity or amusement renders the drink prohibited, and this condition applies universally to all permissible drinks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is drinking date/raisin nabidh. The default ruling in the Hanafi school is mubah (permissible) provided conditions are met.
Nabidh is a drink made by soaking dates or raisins in water, often boiled slightly.
2
Note the first condition: non-intoxication
The nabidh must not be intoxicating. Even if it becomes strong, as long as it does not lead to intoxication, it is permissible.
Intoxication (sukr) is determined by loss of control, not just strength.
3
Note the second condition: intention of the drinker
The drinker must not intend frivolity (lahw) and amusement (tarab). If one intends piety, it is fine; if one intends amusement, it becomes haram by consensus of all imams.
This condition is based on the general principle that acts are judged by intentions.
4
Apply the condition beyond nabidh
The intention condition is not exclusive to nabidh. Drinking any permissible substance (even water) with frivolous intent in the manner of sinners is prohibited.
The commentary in 'al-Ghurar' extends this ruling to all permissible drinks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونبيذ التمر والزبيب مطبوخاً أدنى طبخة وإن اشتدّ إذا شرب ما لم يسكر بلا لهو وطرب ) : أي إنما يحلّ هذه الأشربة إذا شرب ما لم يسكر ، [۲] قوله : بلا لهو وطرب ؛ أي لا يقصد اللهو والطرب ، هذا بخلاف فيما إذا قصد به التقوى ، أما إذا قصد به التلهي والتلعب لا يحلّ باتفاق الأئمة كلها، وهذا القيد لا يختص بهذه الأشربة، بل إذا شرب الماء وغيره من المباحات بلهو وطرب على هيئة الفسقة حرّم. كذا في «الغرر»).
And nabidh of dates and raisins, boiled the slightest boiling, even if it becomes strong, if drunk without intoxication, without frivolity and amusement): Meaning, these drinks are only permissible if drunk without intoxication. [2] His words: 'without frivolity and amusement' meaning that one does not intend frivolity and amusement. This is in contrast to when one intends piety by it. As for when one intends amusement and play, it is not permissible by consensus of all imams. This condition is not specific to these drinks; rather, if one drinks water or other permissible things with frivolity and amusement in the manner of transgressors, it is prohibited. Thus in 'al-Ghurar'.)
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the ruling of date and raisin nabidh. The matn states that such nabidh, even if boiled only slightly and becomes strong, is permissible as long as it is drunk without reaching intoxication and without the intention of frivolity (lahw) and amusement (tarab). The commentary explains that the condition 'without frivolity and amusement' means that one's intention is not to seek entertainment; if one intends piety, it is fine, but if one intends amusement, it is prohibited by consensus of all imams. This condition is not exclusive to these drinks; even drinking water in a sinful manner with frivolity is haram. Thus, the ruling emphasizes both the physical condition (non-intoxication) and the intentional condition (no frivolity).

Key Terms
نبيذ (nabidh): A drink made from dates, raisins, or grapes, often slightly boiled.
لهو وطرب (lahw wa tarab): Frivolity and amusement; engaging in playful behavior while drinking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is permissible because the drink is not intoxicating.
B) It is prohibited by consensus of all imams.
C) It is makruh but not haram.
D) It is permissible only if the person is in a state of piety.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of nabidh hinges on two conditions: it must not intoxicate, and the drinker must not intend frivolity; the latter condition applies to all permissible drinks.

The General Ruling on Nabidh: Conditions for Permissibility
Ruling on permissible drinks with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for permissible nabidh (non-intoxicating and without frivolity) and explain why each is necessary.

Core Idea
Nabidh is permissible if not intoxicating and consumed without frivolity; intention matters.

This lesson examines the foundational ruling on nabidh (infused date or raisin drink). The text establishes that nabidh, even if strong, is permissible as long as it does not cause intoxication and is not consumed with frivolity and amusement. This ruling forms the basis for the later discussion on muthallath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is drinking nabidh (infusion of dates or raisins, cooked slightly). The ruling is mubah (permissible) when two conditions are met.
Nabidh is a common beverage in the Hanafi tradition.
2
List the conditions
Condition 1: The drink must not intoxicate (even if it becomes strong). Condition 2: It must be consumed without the intention of frivolity and amusement (lahw wa tarab).
The condition 'without frivolity' applies to all drinks, including water.
3
Understand the condition of intention
The commentator explains that drinking even permissible liquids with the intention of frivolity is haram by consensus. The intention behind drinking matters legally.
This is a general principle in Hanafi law: al-umur bi-maqasidiha.
4
Apply the ruling
If nabidh is intoxicating or consumed for amusement, it becomes haram. Otherwise, it remains permissible.
This ruling is the standard Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونبيذ التمر والزبيب مطبوخاً أدنى طبخة وإن اشتدّ إذا شرب ما لم يسكر بلا لهو وطرب
وإنما حل المثلث عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف الله لا خلافاً لمحمد ومالك والشافعي (1) .
(1) قوله : وإنما حلّ المثلث الخ ؛ لهما قوله : «حرمت الخمر بعينها قليلها وكثيرها ، والسكر من كلِّ شراب خص السكر بالتحريم في غير الخمر، إذ العطف للمغايرة ؛ ولأنَّ المفسد هو القدح المسكر، وهو حرام عندنا، لا يقال : فحينئذٍ ينبغي أن لا يكون الحرام من الخمر إلا لقدح الأخير.
لأنا نقول : نعم ؛ القياس ذلك ، ولكن تركناه ؛ لأنَّ الخمر لرقتها ولطافتها تدعو إلى الكثير، فأعطي للقليل حكم الكثير ، والمثلث ليس كذلك ؛ لأنَّ غلظه لا يدعو إليه ، بل هو في نفسه غذاء، فبقي على الإباحة.
وأدلة الثلاثة الأخيرة قوله : «كل مسكر حرام»، وقوله : «ما أسكر كثيره فقليله حرام ) ، وقوله : (ما أسكر الجرة فالجرعة منه حرام ) ؛ ولأنَّ المسكر تفسدُ العقل فيكون حراماً قليله وكثيره كالخمر ، وروى عن محمد له مثل قولهما، وعنه أنه كره ذلك ، وعنه أنه قال : لا أحرمه ولا أبيحه. والله أعلم.
[۲]قوله : بلا لهو وطرب ؛ أي لا يقصد اللهو والطرب ، هذا بخلاف فيما إذا قصد به التقوى ، أما إذا قصد به التلهي والتلعب لا يحلّ باتفاق الأئمة كلها، وهذا القيد لا يختص بهذه الأشربة، بل إذا شرب الماء وغيره من المباحات بلهو وطرب على هيئة الفسقة حرّم. كذا في «الغرر»).
And the infusion of dates and raisins, cooked the slightest cooking, and even if it becomes strong, when drunk as long as it does not intoxicate, without frivolity and amusement. And the muthallath is only permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf - may Allah have mercy on them - unlike Muhammad, Malik, and al-Shafi'i. (1) (1) He said: 'And the muthallath is only permissible...' - their proof is his saying: 'Khamr itself is forbidden, its little and its much, and intoxication from every drink' - He specified intoxication as prohibited in other than khamr, because the conjunction indicates differentiation; and because the corrupting element is the intoxicating cup, and it is forbidden according to us. It cannot be said: then the forbidden from khamr should only be the last cup. We say: Yes, that is the analogy, but we abandoned it because khamr, due to its delicacy and subtlety, invites to much, so the ruling of much was given to little, whereas muthallath is not like that because its thickness does not invite to it; rather, it is in itself nutritious, so it remains on permissibility. And the proofs of the latter three are his saying: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden', and his saying: 'Whatever intoxicates in large quantity, a small amount of it is forbidden', and his saying: 'Whatever intoxicates in a jar, a single gulp of it is forbidden'; and because the intoxicant corrupts the intellect, so its little and much are forbidden like khamr. And it is narrated from Muhammad like their statement, and from him that he disliked that, and from him that he said: I do not forbid it nor permit it. And Allah knows best. [2] He said: 'without frivolity and amusement' - meaning he does not intend frivolity and amusement; this is contrary to when he intends obedience. But if he intends amusement and play, it is not permissible by agreement of all imams. And this condition is not specific to these drinks; rather, if one drinks water or other permissible things with frivolity and amusement in the manner of sinners, it is forbidden. Thus in 'al-Ghurar'.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about the permissibility of muthallath (a drink made by cooking date and raisin juice until it reduces to one third). The opening statement rules that nabidh of dates and raisins is permissible if cooked slightly and not intoxicating, and consumed without frivolity. The commentator then addresses the specific case of muthallath, which is a concentrated form. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold it permissible, while Muhammad al-Shaybani has three narrations: one agreeing, one considering it disliked, and one suspending judgment. Malik and al-Shafi'i forbid it. The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'Khamr itself is forbidden' which distinguishes khamr (specifically from grapes and dates) from other intoxicants. The opposing school's proof uses the general hadith 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. The Hanafi response is that the hadith 'intoxication from every drink' specifies that only the intoxicating amount is forbidden in non-khamr drinks, and muthallath's thickness prevents excessive consumption. The condition of 'without frivolity and amusement' is emphasized as a general rule applying to all drinks, including water, if consumed in a sinful manner.

Key Terms
نبيذ (nabidh): An infusion (soaked fruit) in water often slightly fermented but not intoxicating.
لهو وطرب (lahw wa tarab): Frivolity and amusement; drinking with the intention of entertainment and pleasure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what two conditions must be met for nabidh to be permissible?
A) It must be cooked thoroughly and drunk cold.
B) It must not intoxicate and must be consumed without frivolity.
C) It must be made from dates only and drunk in small amounts.
D) It must be non-fermented and consumed with meals.

Key Takeaway
Nabidh is mubah if non-intoxicating and consumed without frivolous intention; intention is a decisive factor.

The Muthallath Dispute: Hanafi Permissibility vs. General Prohibition
Inter-madhhab dispute on the permissibility of muthallath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on muthallath, state the proofs from both sides, and identify the intra-Hanafi variance.

Core Idea
Muthallath is halal according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf because its thickness prevents intoxication, unlike khamr.

This lesson explores the inter-madhhab dispute over muthallath, a concentrated drink from dates and raisins cooked until two-thirds evaporate. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit it, while Imam Muhammad has three narrations, and Malik and al-Shafi'i forbid it. The disagreement stems from interpreting hadith on intoxicants and the concept of istihsan (juristic preference).

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the Hanafi position
The issue is the permissibility of muthallath (concentrated date-raisin drink). The Hanafi position: halal according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
State the opposing positions
Muhammad al-Shaybani has three narrations: (1) halal like the two, (2) makruh, (3) suspended. Malik and al-Shafi'i forbid it entirely.
Intra-Hanafi variance is significant.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Hanafi proof: hadith 'Khamr itself is forbidden... and intoxication from every drink'. The conjunction 'and' differentiates: khamr is forbidden in any amount, while other drinks are only forbidden when they intoxicate. Muthallath is thick and does not invite excess, so it remains permissible.
The Hanafi response to the opposing proof uses istihsan: they abandon qiyas because of the difference in quality.
4
Analyze the opposing proof
Opponents cite 'Every intoxicant is haram' and 'What intoxicates in large quantity, a small amount is haram'. They analogize muthallath to khamr because both are intoxicating in large amounts.
This is a straightforward qiyas.
5
Understand the Hanafi response and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Hanafi response: khamr is thin and invites excess, so its small amount is given the same ruling as much. Muthallath is thick and nutritious, not inviting excess, so it remains on original permissibility. Muhammad's varying opinions reflect caution.
The relied-upon position is the permissibility of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Source Text

مثلث (muthallath): A drink made by cooking date and raisin juice until two-thirds evaporate, leaving one third.
نبيذ (nabidh): An infusion (soaked fruit) in water often slightly fermented but not intoxicating.

The Ruling on Khalitan and Various Nabidh Drinks
Rulings on permissible mixtures and various nabidh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khalitan and other nabidh, identify the proofs, and apply the distinction between permissible non-intoxicating amounts and prohibited intoxicating amounts.

Core Idea
Only intoxicating amounts of non-khamr drinks are haram; permissibility is the default.

This passage addresses two categories of drinks: khalitan (a cooked mixture of date and raisin infusion) and other nabidh (infusions of honey, fig, wheat, barley, corn). The Hanafi school holds them permissible unless they intoxicate. The commentator resolves an apparent contradiction with a hadith prohibiting mixing dates and raisins by contextualizing that prohibition to times of scarcity. He also relies on the hadith restricting khamr to grapevine and date palm to argue that other infusions are not inherently khamr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text treats khalitan (mixed date-raisin infusion) separately from other nabidh (honey, fig, grain infusions). Understand what each category includes.
Khalitan specifically means the mixture of dates and raisins steeped and cooked together.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
Both are permissible (mubah) when not intoxicating and without frivolity. The intoxicating portion is haram. Note that the school of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permits them.
Imam Muhammad's view is not mentioned here; the mufta bihi is permissibility.
3
Examine the proof for khalitan
The proof is the practice of Ibn Umar, who gave a strong but non-intoxicating drink from dates and raisins. The apparent conflict with the hadith forbidding mixing is resolved: that prohibition was for times of hardship, to discourage hoarding, not an absolute ban.
This contextual interpretation is attributed to Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and recorded in Mabsut Shaykh al-Islam.
4
Examine the proof for other nabidh
The proof is the hadith: 'Khamr is from these two trees' (grapevine and date palm). By restricting khamr to two sources, other infusions are not inherently khamr and are permissible unless they intoxicate.
The commentator emphasizes this is a ruling (hukm) not a linguistic definition.
5
Apply the intoxicating principle
The haram element is the last cup that causes intoxication, determined by certainty or overwhelming probability. Drinking below that threshold is halal.
This aligns with the Hanafi rule that only the intoxicating amount of a non-khamr drink is prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخليطان، ونبيذ العسل والتينِ والبُرِّ والشعير والذرة وإن لم يطبخ بلا لهو ولا طرب
[١]قوله : والخليطان ؛ أي يحلّ الخليطان ؛ لما روي عن ابن زياد أنه قال : سقاني ابن عمر شربة ما كدت أهتدي إلى أهلي، فغدوت إليه من الغد فأخبرته بذلك ، فقال : ما زدنـا علـى عجوة وزبيب، وهذا من الخليطين، وكان مطبوخاً ؛ لأنَّ المروي ، حرمة نقيع الزبيب، وهو المنهي عنه. فإن قيل : إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن الجمع بين التمر والزبيب، وعن الرطب والبسر، فكيف يحل.
قلنا : النهي محمول على حالة العسر والقحط، حيث كُرِهَ للأغنياء الجمع بين النعمتين، بل يستحب أن يأكل أحدهما ، ويؤثر الآخر على جاره ، حتى لا يشبع هو وجاره جائع، وما روينا من الإباحة محمول على حالة اليسر والسعة بين الناس، حيث أباحَ الجمع بين النعمتين هكذا مروي عن إبراهيم النخعي . كذا في «مبسوط شيخ الإسلام».
[٢]قوله : ونبيذ العسل.... الخ ؛ أي نبيذ هذه الأشياء حلال، وهو مذهب أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف له ؛ لقوله : «الخمر من هاتين الشجرتين»، وأشار إلى الكرمة والنخلة، خص التحريم بهما. والمراد بيان الحكم إذ هو اللائقُ بمنصب الرسالة، لا بيان اللغة، كما ذهب إليه (1) أي يحرم القدر المسكر منه، وهو الذي يعلم يقيناً أو بغالب الرأي أنه يسكره، فالحرام : هو القدح الأخير الذي يحصل السكر بشربه. ينظر : رد المحتار (٦: ٤٥٣).
(۲) في «صحيح مسلم»(٣: ١٥٧٣)، وغيره.
And the mixed one (khalitan), and the infusion of honey, fig, wheat, barley, and corn, even if not cooked, without frivolity and amusement. [1] He said: 'And the mixed one (khalitan)' - meaning the mixed one is permissible; because of what is narrated from Ibn Ziyad that he said: Ibn Umar gave me a drink such that I almost could not find my way to my family. So I went to him the next day and told him that, and he said: We added nothing but dates and raisins, and this is from the mixed one, and it was cooked; because the narrated (hadith) is about the prohibition of the infusion of raisins, which is forbidden. If it is said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade combining dates and raisins, and combining fresh dates and unripe dates, so how is it permissible? We say: The prohibition is interpreted as referring to times of hardship and famine, where it was disliked for the rich to combine two blessings, rather it is recommended to eat one and prefer the other for his neighbor, so that he does not become full while his neighbor is hungry; and what we narrated of permissibility is interpreted as referring to times of ease and abundance among people, where combining the two blessings is permitted. This is narrated from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. Thus in 'Mabsut Shaykh al-Islam'. [2] He said: 'And the infusion of honey... etc.' - meaning the infusion of these things is permissible, and it is the school of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf - may Allah have mercy on them - because of his saying: 'Khamr is from these two trees', pointing to the grapevine and the date palm, he restricted prohibition to them. The intent is to state the ruling, as is befitting the station of prophethood, not to define language, as he held (1) meaning the intoxicating amount is forbidden, which is known with certainty or by overwhelming opinion that it will intoxicate; so the forbidden is the last cup which causes intoxication when drunk. See: Radd al-Muhtar (6:453). (2) In Sahih Muslim (3:1573), etc.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage rules on two categories: (1) al-khalitan (the mixture of date and raisin infusion) and (2) other nabidh (from honey, fig, wheat, barley, corn). Both are permissible when not intoxicating and without frivolity. The commentator explains that khalitan is halal based on the practice of Ibn Umar, who gave a strong but permissible drink. The apparent conflict with the hadith forbidding mixing dates and raisins is resolved by interpreting that prohibition as specific to times of scarcity, to prevent hoarding blessings, while in times of abundance it is allowed. As for other nabidh, they are halal by the school of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, relying on the hadith that khamr (wine) is specifically from grapevines and date palms, thus other fruits are not inherently prohibited. The prohibition only applies to the intoxicating amount, which is determined by the last cup that causes intoxication. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that only intoxicating quantities of non-khamr drinks are haram.

Key Terms
خليطان (khalitan): A mixture of date and raisin infusion cooked together.
نبيذ العسل (nabidh al-asal): An infusion of honey in water, often slightly fermented.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Hanafi permissibility of nabidh made from honey or grain?
A) The hadith 'Khamr is from these two trees' restricts khamr to grapevine and date palm.
B) The practice of the Companions.
C) It is not mentioned in the Quran.
D) Qiyas with water.

Key Takeaway
Khalitan and other nabidh are permissible as long as they do not intoxicate; the intoxicating amount alone is haram.

The Permissibility of Al-Khalitan (Mixed Date and Raisin Drink)
Commentator explains permissibility of al-khalitan and answers objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on al-khalitan, cite the evidence from Ibn Umar, and explain the response to the objection regarding the prohibition of mixing dates and raisins.

Core Idea
Al-khalitan is permissible when cooked and without amusement; the prohibition was for times of scarcity.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary clarifies that mixing dates and raisins to make an intoxicating drink (al-khalitan) is permissible under certain conditions. The author cites a hadith of Ibn Umar as proof, then addresses an apparent contradiction with another hadith that forbids mixing dates and raisins. The resolution lies in contextual interpretation: the prohibition was specific to times of hardship, encouraging generosity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
What is al-khalitan? According to the matn, it is permissible (halal) when cooked and without amusement.
2
Locate the proof
The commentary narrates an incident with Ibn Umar, who gave such a drink and considered it permissible.
The hadith shows practical application by a Companion.
3
Understand the objection
The opponent cites a general prohibition from the Prophet against mixing dates and raisins.
This objection is based on a hadith that seems to forbid such mixtures.
4
Apply the Hanafi interpretive method
The prohibition is confined to times of scarcity (hardship), while permissibility applies in times of ease. This is supported by the narration from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.
This is an example of takhsis (specification) of a general rule.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
The Hanafi position is that al-khalitan is mubah when conditions are met, with the apparent conflict resolved through contextual interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخليطان، ونبيذ العسل والتينِ والبُرِّ والشعير والذرة وإن لم يطبخ بلا لهو ولا طرب (والخليطان)) : وهو أن يجمع بين ماء التمر والزبيب ويطبخ أدنى طبخة ، ويترك إلى أن يغلي ويشتد، يحل بلا لهو وطرب. قوله : والخليطان ; أي يحلّ الخليطان ; لما روي عن ابن زياد أنه قال : سقاني ابن عمر شربة ما كدت أهتدي إلى أهلي، فغدوت إليه من الغد فأخبرته بذلك ، فقال : ما زدنـا علـى عجوة وزبيب، وهذا من الخليطين، وكان مطبوخاً ; لأنَّ المروي ، حرمة نقيع الزبيب، وهو المنهي عنه. فإن قيل : إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن الجمع بين التمر والزبيب، وعن الرطب والبسر، فكيف يحل. قلنا : النهي محمول على حالة العسر والقحط، حيث كُرِهَ للأغنياء الجمع بين النعمتين، بل يستحب أن يأكل أحدهما ، ويؤثر الآخر على جاره ، حتى لا يشبع هو وجاره جائع، وما روينا من الإباحة محمول على حالة اليسر والسعة بين الناس، حيث أباحَ الجمع بين النعمتين هكذا مروي عن إبراهيم النخعي . كذا في «مبسوط شيخ الإسلام».
And al-khalitan, and nabidh of honey, figs, wheat, barley, and corn even if not cooked without amusement and entertainment. (Al-khalitan): It is to combine water of dates and raisins and cook it a minimal cooking, and leave it until it boils and becomes strong, it is permissible without amusement and entertainment. (Commentary): He said: 'And al-khalitan'; that is, al-khalitan is permissible; based on what was narrated from Ibn Ziyad that he said: Ibn Umar gave me a drink that nearly made me unable to find my family, so I went to him the next morning and informed him of that, and he said: We only added a date and a raisin, and this is from al-khalitan, and it was cooked; because what is narrated is the prohibition of raisin infusion, and it is what is forbidden. If it is said: The Prophet (s) forbade combining dates and raisins, and fresh dates and unripe dates, so how is it permissible? We say: The prohibition is interpreted as during hardship and famine, where it is disliked for the rich to combine the two blessings, rather it is recommended to eat one and give the other to his neighbor, so that he does not become full while his neighbor is hungry. And what we narrated of permissibility is interpreted as during ease and plenty among people, where combining the two blessings is permitted. Thus narrated from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. As in Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The matn states that the mixture of dates and raisins (al-khalitan) is permissible without amusement, provided it is cooked minimally until it becomes strong. The commentary expands on this by citing a hadith from Ibn Umar, who gave such a drink and confirmed it was only a mixture of dates and raisins. The commentator then addresses an objection based on a hadith prohibiting combining dates and raisins. The response explains that prohibition was specific to times of scarcity to encourage sharing, while permissibility applies in times of abundance. This interpretation is attributed to Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and found in the Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam. Thus, the Hanafi position is that al-khalitan is halal when conditions are met.

Key Terms
الخليطان (al-khalitan): A mixture of two types of drinks, specifically water of dates and water of raisins, cooked lightly and allowed to ferment until strong.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Haram
B) Makruh

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits al-khalitan when cooked and without amusement, interpreting the prohibition as specific to times of scarcity.

Permissibility of Nabidh from Honey, Figs, and Grains
Commentator states permissibility of nabidh from various sources according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nabidh from honey, figs, and grains, and explain the proof from the hadith 'al-khamr min hatayn al-shajaratayn'.

Core Idea
Nabidh from sources other than grapes and dates is permissible; the hadith restricts khamr to those two trees.

This lesson clarifies a key Hanafi distinction: not every infusion (nabidh) is khamr. The Prophet's hadith explicitly limits khamr to grape and date sources. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf thus permit nabidh from honey, figs, wheat, barley, and corn, provided it is consumed without amusement or intoxication. The commentator emphasizes that the hadith is a ruling, not a linguistic definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn and commentary state that nabidh from honey, figs, wheat, barley, and corn is permissible (halal). The ruling applies even if the infusion is not cooked, as long as it is free from amusement (lahw) and entertainment (tarab).
The prohibition of khamr is specific; this nabidh is not khamr.
2
Locate the condition
The condition 'bila lahw wa la tarab' (without amusement or entertainment) indicates that the permissibility is contingent on the drink not being consumed in a sinful manner. If it becomes intoxicating, it would be prohibited regardless.
The ruling assumes the drink does not intoxicate in itself; if it does, it becomes khamr.
3
Examine the proof
The proof is the prophetic hadith: 'Al-khamr min hatayn al-shajaratayn' (Khamr comes from these two trees), with 'these two trees' being the grapevine and the date palm. This is a Sunnah ahad but accepted by the school.
The hadith is cited in Sahih Muslim.
4
Understand the Hanafi interpretation
The commentary explains that the hadith is a statement of ruling (hukm), not a linguistic definition. It restricts the prohibition of khamr to grapes and dates. Other substances are not inherently khamr; their infusions are permissible unless they become intoxicating.
Some scholars interpret the hadith as a linguistic definition, but the Hanafi school adopts the ruling interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونبيذ العسل والتينِ والبُرِّ والشعير والذرة وإن لم يطبخ بلا لهو ولا طرب قوله : ونبيذ العسل.... الخ ; أي نبيذ هذه الأشياء حلال، وهو مذهب أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف له ; لقوله : «الخمر من هاتين الشجرتين»، وأشار إلى الكرمة والنخلة، خص التحريم بهما. والمراد بيان الحكم إذ هو اللائقُ بمنصب الرسالة، لا بيان اللغة، كما ذهب إليه
And nabidh of honey, figs, wheat, barley, and corn even if not cooked without amusement and entertainment. He said: 'And nabidh of honey... etc.'; that is, the nabidh of these things is permissible, and it is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf for him; due to his saying: 'Khamr comes from these two trees,' and he pointed to the grapevine and the date palm, specifically prohibiting those two. And the intended meaning is the statement of the ruling, as it befits the position of prophethood, not a statement of language, as he held.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The matn declares that nabidh (infusion) of honey, figs, wheat, barley, and corn is permissible even if not cooked, provided it is without amusement. The commentary identifies this as the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The proof is the Prophet's hadith, 'Khamr comes from these two trees,' (grapevine and date palm), which restricts the prohibition of khamr to these specific sources. Thus, infusions from other ingredients are not inherently khamr and are permissible. The commentator clarifies that the hadith is stating a ruling, not a linguistic definition, contrary to the view of some.

Key Terms
نبيذ (nabidh): An infusion or drink made by steeping ingredients like honey, figs, or grains in water, which may or may not become intoxicating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the evidence used by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf for the permissibility of nabidh from honey and grains?
A) The consensus of the Sahaba
B) The hadith that 'Khamr comes from these two trees' (grapevine and date palm)
C) The analogy between honey and date syrup
D) A verse from the Quran

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits nabidh from honey, figs, and grains based on the hadith that restricts khamr to grapes and dates, provided the drink does not become intoxicating and is consumed without amusement.

Vinegar from Wine: Transformational Permissibility
Commentator states permissibility of vinegar from wine even if treated, refuting Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vinegar from wine and explain why it differs from the Shafi'i position, applying the hadith and analogy.

Core Idea
Transformation removes prohibition; vinegar from wine is halal absolutely.

This lesson explores a classical fiqh dispute: the permissibility of vinegar made from wine. The Hanafi school holds it absolutely permissible, including the process of making it, while the Shafi'i school has conditions. We'll examine the proofs and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What does the Hanafi school rule on vinegar from wine and the process of making it (takhleel)? Extract the core ruling from the text.
The text says 'خل الخمر حلال مطلقاً عندنا'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school differs and on what grounds? Note the distinction between natural and induced transformation according to the opponent.
Imam al-Shafi'i holds two opinions; if induced, it is impermissible in his single opinion.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is the Shafi'i argument (dalil) against takhleel? Understand their reasoning about drawing near to wine.
They cite the command to avoid wine (Quran 5:90).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
What proof does the Hanafi school present? Look at the hadith and the analogy to tanning and natural vinegar.
Hadith: 'نعم الإدام الخل' and the removal of the corrupting quality.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the two positions in daily life? How does this affect a Muslim's actions?
Hanafis may intentionally make vinegar from wine; Shafi'is may not if treatment is added.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخل الخمر ولو بعلاج ) : أي بإلقاء شيء فيه، وهذا احتراز عن قول الشافعي ، فإنّ التخليل إذا كان بإلقاء شيءٍ لا يحل الخل قولاً واحداً، وإن كان بغير إلقاء شيء ففيه قولان له. قوله : وخل الخمر ; ولو بعلاج ; أي خل الخمر حلال مطلقاً عندنا، وكذا التخليل خلافاً للشافعي له ; له : إنَّ في التخليل اقتراباً من الخمر على وجه التموّل، والأمر بالاجتناب ينافيه. ولنا : قوله : نعم الإدام الخل» ; ولأن بالتخليل يزولُ الوصف المفسد ، وتثبت صفة الصلاح من حيث تسكين الصفراء، وكسر الشهوة، والتغذي به، والإصلاح مباح ، وكذا الصالح للمصالح اعتبار بالمتخلل بنفسه وبالدباغ.
And vinegar of wine even if by treatment): i.e., by throwing something into it. This is a precaution against the statement of al-Shafi'i, for if the making into vinegar is by throwing something into it, then the vinegar is not permissible in his single opinion, and if it is without throwing something, then there are two opinions for him. He said: 'And vinegar of wine, even if by treatment'; i.e., vinegar of wine is absolutely permissible according to us, and likewise making it into vinegar, contrary to al-Shafi'i. For him (Shafi'i): Making it into vinegar is drawing near to wine in a way of seeking wealth, and the command to avoid wine contradicts it. For us: His saying: 'Excellent as a condiment is vinegar'; and because by making into vinegar, the corrupting quality disappears, and the good quality is established in terms of calming bile, breaking desire, and nourishing with it, and correction is permissible, and likewise what is good for beneficial purposes by analogy to naturally occurring vinegar and to tanning.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The matn states that vinegar from wine is permissible even if treated. The commentary explains that this phrase excludes the view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that if vinegar is made by adding something, it is not permissible in any case; if no addition, there are two opinions. The Hanafi position (ours) is that vinegar from wine is absolutely permissible, as is the process of making it. The Shafi'i argument is that making vinegar involves approaching wine for profit, which contradicts the command to avoid wine. Hanafi responds with the hadith, 'Excellent as a condiment is vinegar,' and reasoned that the corrupting property (alcohol) is removed and beneficial properties are established. This is analogous to naturally occurring vinegar and the purification of animal skins through tanning.

Key Terms
تخليل (takhleel): The process of turning wine into vinegar, either by adding something or by natural change.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on vinegar made from wine by adding something (e.g., salt or onion)?
A) It is halal
B) It is haram
C) It is makruh
D) It depends on the intention

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the complete transformation (istihala) of wine into vinegar removes its prohibition, and the process of making it is allowed.

Ruling on Steeping in Prohibited Vessels (Al-Intibadh)
Commentator explains the prohibition on certain vessels was abrogated, steeping is now permissible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on using previously prohibited vessels for steeping, identify the evidence of abrogation, and explain the intra-school dispute regarding cooking.

Core Idea
The prohibition on steeping in dubba, hantam, muzaffat, and naqir was abrogated; use is now permissible (mubah).

The early Muslim community was prohibited from using certain vessels for steeping because of their association with wine. However, the Prophet (s) later abrogated this prohibition, allowing their use. This lesson examines the four types of vessels, the reasoning behind the original prohibition, and the evidence for abrogation, along with a later intra-Hanafi difference about whether cooking is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vessels and initial prohibition
List the four vessels (dubba, hantam, muzaffat, naqir) and their definitions. Understand that they were specifically associated with wine, leading to prohibition either because using them resembles wine drinking or because they retained traces of wine.
2
Understand the basis of prohibition
Two reasons are given: to avoid resemblance to wine drinkers, or because the vessels had residual wine traces. The prohibition was thus secondary to the prohibition of wine itself.
3
Learn the abrogation
After a period, the Prophet (s) permitted the use of these vessels. The hadith from Kitab al-Athar of Imam Muhammad illustrates abrogation in other rulings (visiting graves, storing meat), analogically applied here.
The cited hadith is about other abrogations; the commentator uses it to show the principle of naskh.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is a difference among Hanafi scholars: some require cooking the drink in these vessels for permissibility, others do not. Details are in the commentaries of al-Hidayah.
5
Connect to usul al-fiqh: naskh
This case demonstrates the operation of naskh (abrogation) – a later permission overrides an earlier prohibition. The Hanafi school relies on the principle that the final ruling is what is operative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والانتباه في الدباء والحنتم والمزفّتِ والنقير)، الدباء : القرع، والحنتم: الجرة الخضراء، والمزفت : الظرفُ المطلي بالزفت ; أي القير، والنقير: الظرف الذي يكون من الخشب المنقور. اعلم أنَّ هذه الظروف كانت مختصة بالخمر، فإذا حرمت الخمر حرم النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم استعمال هذه الظروف، إما لأنَّ في استعمالها تشبها بشرب الخمر، وإما لأنَّ هذه الظروف كان فيها أثرُ الخمر، فلما مضت مدة أباح النبي ) استعمال هذه مالك والشافعي ، ثم قيل : يشترط فيه الطبخ لإباحته، وقيل: لا يشترط ، وتفصيله في شروح «الهداية». قوله : أباح النبي ، روى محمد الله في كتاب «الآثار» عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : نهيتكم عن زيارة القبور فزوروها، ولا تقولوا هجراً، فقد أذن محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم في زيارة قبر أمه، وعن لحوم الأضاحي لا تمسكوها فوق ثلاثة أيام فامسكواها ما بدا لكم وتزودوا.
And steeping in gourds (dubba), green jars (hantam), pitched vessels (muzaffat), and hollowed wood (naqir). Dubba: gourd, hantam: green jar, muzaffat: container coated with pitch (i.e., tar), naqir: container made from hollowed wood. Know that these vessels were specific to wine. So when wine was prohibited, the Prophet (s) prohibited the use of these vessels, either because using them resembles drinking wine, or because these vessels had traces of wine. Then after a period passed, the Prophet (s) permitted their use. Malik and al-Shafi'i [hold a view?], then it was said: cooking is required for its permissibility, and it was said: not required, and its detail is in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. He said: 'The Prophet permitted' – Muhammad reported in Kitab al-Athar from the Prophet (s) that he said: I forbade you from visiting graves, so now visit them, and do not say obscenities, for Muhammad (s) has permitted visiting the grave of his mother; and regarding the meat of sacrificial animals, do not keep it for more than three days, so now keep it as long as you wish and store it.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The matn mentions steeping in four types of vessels that were initially prohibited: dubba (gourds), hantam (green jars), muzaffat (vessels coated with pitch), and naqir (hollowed wood). The commentary explains that these vessels were associated with wine, so when wine was prohibited, their use was also prohibited to avoid resemblance or due to residual traces. However, this prohibition was later abrogated, and the Prophet (s) permitted their use. The commentator notes that Imam Malik and al-Shafi'i may have different views, and there is a discussion about whether cooking the drink in these vessels is required for permissibility, with details in commentaries of al-Hidayah. A hadith from Kitab al-Athar is cited as evidence of abrogation, though the hadith text mentions other examples of abrogation (visiting graves, storing meat).

Key Terms
الدباء (dubba): Gourds (pumpkins) used as containers for steeping.
الحنتم (hantam): Green clay jars, often used for wine storage.
المزفت (muzaffat): Vessels coated with pitch (tar) to seal them.
النقير (naqir): Hollowed-out wood used as a container.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the current ruling on steeping drinks in gourds (dubba) and green jars (hantam)?
A) It remains prohibited
B) It is permissible because the prohibition was abrogated
C) It is permissible only if the drink is cooked
D) It is makruh tanzihi

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the prohibition on steeping in dubba, hantam, muzaffat, and naqir was abrogated, making such use permissible (mubah) absent other impediments.

Definition and Conditions for Lawful Hunting
Defines sayd and outlines conditions for permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sayd in Hanafi law and identify the conditions (shurut) that make hunting lawful, including the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on jarh.

Core Idea
Hunting is lawful only with a trained animal that wounds—and the five conditions for the dog are the key to its validity.

This lesson explores the opening of Kitab al-Sayd. The commentator defines hunting and outlines the essential conditions for its permissibility. We will focus on the five conditions related to the hunting dog, including the requirement of training and wounding, and an important difference of opinion within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Define sayd
Understand the linguistic meaning (verbal noun for the act) and legal definition (all wild, untamable animals, edible or not).
The term shifts from act to object.
2
Classify the ruling
Identify when hunting is lawful (mubah) and when it becomes unlawful (in ihram or in the Haram).
Outside these prohibitions, conditions apply.
3
List the conditions for the dog
The passage specifies five conditions: trained (mu'allam), wounds (jarh), no sharing capture, no prolonged stop, does not eat from prey.
Only two are detailed in the excerpt; others mentioned briefly.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The condition of jarh is disputed: the zahir al-riwaya requires it, but a narration from Abu Hanifa does not. The mufta bihi is that jarh is required.
This is a legitimate difference within the school.
5
Apply the dalil
The Quranic verse (5:4) 'مَا عَلَّمْتُم مِّنَ الْجَوَارِحِ' is cited to support the condition of training.
The Hanafi interpretation adds jarh based on sunnah/practice.

كتاب الصيد
 يحل صيد كل ذي ناب وذي مخلب من كلب أو باز ونحوهما. قد مر في «الذبائح» معنى ذي الناب وذي المخلب. مناسبة كتاب الصيد بكتاب الأشربة من حيث أن كل واحد من الأشربة والصيد مما يورث السرور إلا أنه قدم الأشربة بحرمتها، اعتباراً بالاحتراز منها ومجانبته ومحاسن المكاسب. الصيد هو مصدر بمعنى الاصطياد، ثم سمي به الصيد تسمية للمفعول بالمصدر، فصار اسماً لكل حيوان متوحش ممتنع عن الآدمي، مأكولاً كان أو غير مأكول، لما فيه من تحصيل المنفعة من جلده أو شعره، أو دفع أذاه من الناس، وهو حلال أو حرام؛ لأن الصائد إما أن يكون محرماً أو لا؛ فإن كان محرماً فهو حرام. وإن لم يكن فإما أن يصطاد في الحرم أو لا، فالأول حرام أيضاً، والثاني فهو حلال إن وجد فيه خمسة عشر شرطاً، خمسة في الكلب: الاثنان منها أن يكون معلماً وأن يقتل جرحاً؛ لقوله: وَمَا عَلَّمْتُم مِّنَ الجوارح . هذا ظاهر الرواية، وعن الأعظم: إنه لا يشترط الجرح، وقد عبر عنهما المصنف بقوله: بشرط علمهما وجرحهما؛ أي علم ذي ناب وذي مخلب وجرحهما. والثالث: أن لا يشاركه في الأخذ ما لا يحل صيده، وقد عبر عنه بقوله: أن لا يشاركه الكلب... الخ. والرابع: أن يذهب على سنن الإرسال وقد عبر عنه بقوله: ولا يطول وقفته. والخامس: أن لا يأكل منه، وقد عبر عنه بقوله: إلا إن أكل الكلب. وخمسة في الصائد: ...
The Book of Hunting. The hunting of every fanged beast and clawed bird such as a dog or falcon and the like is permissible. The meaning of fanged and clawed has been mentioned in the Book of Slaughtered Animals. The appropriateness of placing the Book of Hunting after the Book of Drinks is that both drinks and hunting bring joy, except that he presented drinks with their prohibition, considering avoidance and turning away from them and good earnings. Hunting (al-sayd) is a verbal noun meaning the act of hunting, then it is named 'sayd' by naming the object with the source, so it became a name for every wild animal that is resistant to humans, whether edible or not, because of the benefit obtained from its skin or hair, or repelling its harm from people. It is either lawful or unlawful because the hunter is either in ihram or not; if in ihram, it is unlawful. If not, then either he hunts in the Haram or not; the first is also unlawful, and the second is lawful if fifteen conditions are found: five in the dog: two of them being that it is trained and that it wounds; due to His saying: 'And what you have taught of hunting animals.' This is the apparent narration, and from al-A'zam: that wounding is not required, and the author has expressed both with his saying: 'on condition of their training and wounding'; meaning the training and wounding of the fanged and clawed. The third: that nothing which is not permissible to hunt shares in the capture, and he expressed it with his saying: 'that the dog is not shared... etc.' The fourth: that it goes according to the way of release, and he expressed it with his saying: 'and its stopping does not lengthen.' The fifth: that it does not eat from it, and he expressed it with his saying: 'unless the dog eats.' And five in the hunter: ...
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of Hunting (Kitab al-Sayd) in the commentary. The author begins by stating the general rule: it is permissible to hunt with any fanged beast or clawed bird, such as dogs and falcons. The commentator notes that the meaning of 'fanged' and 'clawed' has been explained earlier in the Book of Slaughtered Animals (Kitab al-Dhaba'ih). He then discusses the arrangement of the book: hunting follows drinks because both bring joy, but the precedence of drinks (with their prohibition) is to emphasize caution and good earnings. A legal definition of 'sayd' is provided: it is originally a verbal noun for the act of hunting, but metonymically used for the animal hunted, hence it refers to any wild, untamable animal, whether edible or not, because of the benefit derived from its skin, hair, or protection from its harm. The ruling on hunting is then classified: it is unlawful for a person in ihram (pilgrim's state) or within the Haram (sanctuary of Mecca). Outside these, it is lawful if fifteen conditions are fulfilled: five relating to the hunting animal (here, the dog) and five to the hunter (the remaining five conditions are not detailed in this excerpt). The conditions for the dog are: (1) it must be trained (mu'allam), (2) it must wound the prey (jarh), (3) no other animal that is not permissible to hunt should share in the capture, (4) the dog must not pause too long after being released, and (5) the dog must not eat from the prey. The author notes a difference of narration: the apparent position (zahir al-riwaya) requires both training and wounding, but it is also reported from Imam Abu Hanifa that wounding is not a condition. The term 'teaching' (ta'lim) for a fanged or clawed animal means training it to obey commands and refrain from eating the prey. The conditions provide a framework for lawful hunting according to Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الصيد (al-sayd): A verbal noun meaning the act of hunting, then used metonymically for the hunted animal. It refers to any wild, untamable animal (whether edible or not) from which benefit can be derived.
المعلم (al-mu'allam): A trained hunting animal (dog or bird) that responds to commands and refrains from eating the prey, enabling the hunter to benefit from its kill.
الجرح (al-jarh): Wounding inflicted by the hunting animal on the prey, typically causing death through a tear or bite. According to the apparent position of the school, this is a condition for the kill to be lawful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a condition for the hunting dog in Hanafi law?
A) The dog must be male.
B) The dog must be trained (mu'allam) and must wound the prey.
C) The dog must be owned by the hunter.
D) The dog must be fed before hunting.

Key Takeaway
Hunting is lawful only when the animal is trained and wounds, and the hunter is not in ihram or in the Haram; the five conditions for the dog are essential.

Definition of Sayd and Its Fifteen Conditions
Defines sayd and lists conditions for hunting animal, hunter, and game.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of sayd in Hanafi fiqh, list the fifteen conditions for lawful hunting, and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the requirement of wounding.

Core Idea
Hunting is permissible with trained animals that wound, but only under fifteen conditions — five for the animal, five for the hunter, and five for the game.

In this opening of Kitab al-Sayd, the commentator defines 'sayd' and lays out a comprehensive structure of conditions that make hunting lawful. Understanding these conditions is essential for distinguishing permissible from impermissible hunting. The passage also reveals a key intra-Hanafi dispute over whether the hunting animal must physically wound the prey.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of sayd
Identify the linguistic and terminological meaning of sayd: verbal noun for hunting, also used for the prey itself. Note that it includes both edible and inedible wild animals that resist humans, as benefit can be derived.
This broad definition is distinct from some other schools.
2
Identify the three groups of conditions
The passage divides fifteen conditions into three sets of five: conditions relating to the hunting animal (e.g., dog), conditions relating to the hunter, and conditions relating to the game.
Memorizing this tripartite structure helps in analysis.
3
Analyse the conditions for the hunting animal
List the five conditions: trained (mu'allam), wounds (jarh), no sharing with an impermissible animal, follows release without long pause, does not eat from prey. Note the proof from Quran 5:4.
Abu Hanifa does not require wounding; the apparent narration requires it.
4
Analyse the conditions for the hunter and the game
For the hunter: release, being Muslim or Kitabi, no sharing in release with Magian, not omitting tasmiyah intentionally, no other action between release and taking. For the game: wild and resisting, not insects, not sea creatures (except fish), not a predator that hunts with fangs/claws, dies from the attack before slaughter.
The game conditions ensure the hunt is on a permissible target.
5
Recognise the exceptions and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note the exception for pig (impure) and the views of Abu Yusuf (lion/bear cannot be trained). Understand that the condition of wounding is from the apparent narration, while Abu Hanifa does not require it.
The mufta bihi is the condition of wounding.

Book of Hunting: The hunting of every fanged animal and every clawed animal, whether dog or falcon or the like, is permissible. (The meaning of fanged and clawed has already passed in 'The Book of Slaughtered Animals.') (1) His statement: Book of Hunting; the appropriateness of the Book of Hunting with the Book of Beverages is that each of beverages and hunting brings joy, except that he gave precedence to beverages due to their prohibition, considering precaution against them and avoiding them and good earnings. So in al-'Inayah. (2) His statement: al-Sayd; it is a verbal noun meaning hunting, then the prey is called sayd by naming the object with the verbal noun, so it became a name for every wild animal that resists humans, whether edible or inedible, because of the benefit obtained from its skin or hair, or repelling its harm from people, and it is permissible or forbidden; because the hunter is either in ihram or not: if in ihram, it is forbidden. If not, then either he hunts in the Haram or not: the first is also forbidden, and the second is permissible if fifteen conditions are found, five in the dog: two of them are that it be trained and that it wound; because of His statement: 'And what you have taught of hunting animals' (Quran 5:4) the verse. This is the apparent narration, and from al-A'zam (Abu Hanifa): that wounding is not a condition, and the author (al-Quduri) expressed both by saying: 'on condition of their being trained and their wounding'; meaning the training of the fanged and the clawed and their wounding. The third: that what does not permit its hunting does not share with it in taking, and he expressed it by saying: 'that the dog does not share... etc.' The fourth: that it goes according to the manner of release, and he expressed it by saying: 'and its pause does not become long.' The fifth: that it does not eat from it, and he expressed it by saying: 'except if the dog eats.' And five in the hunter: Then know that the pig is excepted because it is impure in essence, and Abu Yusuf excepted the lion because of its high ambition, and the bear because of its baseness, and some attached the kite (a bird) to it due to its baseness, and the apparent view is that there is no need for exception; for the lion and the bear do not become trained due to high ambition and baseness, so the condition for permissible hunting is not found. 1. That release occur from it. 2. That he be of those who slaughter, i.e., Muslim or Kitabi. 3. That one whose hunting is not permissible, like a Magian, does not share with him in the release. 4. That he not omit the tasmiyah intentionally, and he expressed these four by saying: 'and the release of a Muslim for them both while saying Bismillah.' And the fifth: that he not occupy himself between release and taking with another action, and he expressed it by saying: 'and that he does not sit back from seeking it... etc.' And five in the game: The first: that it prevent itself from his intent either with its legs or wings, so domestic animals are excluded, and he expressed it by saying: 'upon a resisting wild animal.' The second: that it not be of insects. The third: that it not be of sea animals, except fish. The fourth: that it not be an aggressor or hunter with its fangs or claws, and he expressed these three by saying: 'it is eaten because nothing of them is eaten.' The fifth: that it die by that before it reaches its slaughter, and I did not find it explicitly in the matn, although some of its wording is not devoid of indication towards it in general.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Hunting from the commentary on Al-Hidayah. It begins by stating the basic ruling: it is permissible to hunt with any fanged or clawed animal, such as dogs or falcons. The commentator then explains the connection between the Book of Hunting and the previous Book of Beverages: both bring joy, but beverages were given precedence due to their prohibition, emphasizing caution.

Next, the term 'sayd' is defined: originally a verbal noun meaning 'hunting', it is also used for the prey itself (the object). It applies to any wild animal that resists capture, whether edible or not, as benefit can be derived from its skin, hair, or by repelling harm. The ruling varies: hunting is forbidden for a pilgrim in ihram or within the Haram; otherwise, it is permissible subject to fifteen conditions. These are grouped into five for the hunting animal, five for the hunter, and five for the game.

For the animal (e.g., dog), the first two conditions are that it be trained (mu'allam) and that it wounds (jarh). The proof is Quran 5:4. There is a difference of opinion: Abu Hanifa does not require wounding, but the author (al-Quduri) states both training and wounding as conditions based on the apparent narration. The third condition is that no other animal whose hunting is impermissible shares in the kill. The fourth is that the animal does not pause too long after release. The fifth is that it does not eat from the prey.

For the hunter, conditions include: releasing the animal, being a Muslim or Kitabi (Ahl al-Kitab), no sharing in release with one whose hunting is invalid (e.g., Magian), not omitting the tasmiyah intentionally, and not engaging in another action between release and taking.

For the game, conditions include: being a wild animal that resists (excluding domestic animals), not being an insect, not being a sea creature except fish, not being a predator that itself hunts with fangs/claws (since such animals are not eaten), and dying from the attack before it can be slaughtered.

Finally, exceptions are discussed: the pig is inherently impure. Abu Yusuf adds the lion (due to high ambition) and the bear (due to baseness), but the commentator notes that these cannot actually become trained, so no exception is needed. Some also include the kite bird.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): A verbal noun meaning hunting, also used for the prey itself; a wild animal that resists capture, whether edible or not.
ذي ناب (dhi nāb): An animal with fangs, such as dogs, wolves, etc.
ذي مخلب (dhi mikhlab): An animal with claws, such as falcons, hawks, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Five
B) Four
C) Six
D) Three

Key Takeaway
Hunting is a regulated act in Hanafi fiqh with fifteen detailed conditions ensuring it is performed lawfully and ethically.

Conditions for Permissible Hunting in Hanafi Fiqh
Lists exceptions and conditions for hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid hunt according to the Hanafi school and explain the intra-Hanafi difference on lion and bear.

Core Idea
A valid hunt requires specific conditions in hunter, game, and method; otherwise the meat is not halal.

Hunting is a legitimate means of obtaining food, but Islamic law imposes strict conditions to ensure the animal is lawfully taken. This passage from a Hanafi manual lists the exceptions (animals that can never be hunted) and the conditions that must be fulfilled by the hunter and the game. Understanding these rules is essential for ensuring that hunted meat is permissible to eat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptions
Note which animals are absolutely excluded from permissible hunting: pig (najis al-ayn), lion, bear, and kite (by some views). Understand the reasoning: pig is impure; lion and bear cannot be properly trained (mu'allam) due to high spirit or baseness.
The preferred view is that no explicit exception is needed because training is impossible.
2
List the hunter's conditions
Extract the five conditions for the hunter: (1) sending the animal/projectile, (2) being Muslim or Kitabi, (3) no partner from a non-halal hunter, (4) intentional tasmiyah, (5) no separate action between sending and taking.
If tasmiyah is omitted intentionally, the hunt is invalid.
3
List the game's conditions
Extract the five conditions for the game: (1) wild and inaccessible, (2) not an insect, (3) not a sea creature except fish, (4) not a predator that hunts by fang or claw, (5) must die from the hunt before slaughter.
If the game is caught alive, it must be slaughtered, not treated as hunted.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Recognize that Abu Yusuf explicitly excludes lion and bear; some add the kite. The preferred view (az-zahir) is that no exception is needed because these animals cannot be trained, fulfilling the condition of ta'lim.
This difference does not affect the final ruling but shows method of istinbat.

كتاب الصيد / شروطه وأحكامه
ثم اعلم أنَّ الخنزير مستثنى ؛ لأنه نجس العين، وأبو يوسف استثنى الأسد ؛ لعلو همته ، والدُّبّ ؛ لخساسته ، والبعض ألحق الحدأة) به ؛ لخساسته ، والظاهر أنه لا يحتاج إلى الاستثناء ؛ فإنّ الأسد والدب لا يصيران مُعلمين لعلو الهمة والخساسة، فلم يوجد شرط حلّ الصيد.
1. أن يوجد منه الإرسال.
. وأن يكون من أهل الذبح، بأن يكون مسلماً أو كتابياً.
. وأن لا يشاركه في الإرسال من لا يحل صيده كالمجوسي.
. وأن لا يترك التسمية عامداً ، وقد عبر عن هذه الأربعة بقوله : وإرسال مسلم إياهما مسمياً ه . والخامس : أن لا يشغل بين الإرسال والأخذ بعمل آخر، وقد عبر عنه بقوله : وأن لا يقعد عن طلبه ... الخ.
وخمسة في الصيد :
الأول : أن يمنع نفسه عن قصده إما بقوائمه أو بأجنحته، فيخرج منه الحيوانات الأهلية، وقد عبّر عنه بقوله : على ممتنع متوحش.
والثاني : أن لا يكون من الحشرات.
والثالث : أن لا يكون من الحيوانات البحرية، إلا السمك.
والرابع : أن لا يكون متعديا أو صائداً بأنيابه أو مخلبه، وقد عبر عن هذه الثلاثة بقوله : يؤكل لأنه لا يؤكل شيء منها.
والخامس : أن يموت، بهذا قبل أن يصل إلى ذبحه، وما وجدته في «المتن» صريحاً، وإن كان بعض عبارته لا يخلو عن الإشارة إليه في الجملة.
(۱) حدأة : بالكسر : وهي طائر من الجوارح، وهو أخس الطير يغلبه أكثر الطيور، وينقض على الجرذان والدواجن، والغراب يسرق بيض الحدأة ويترك مكانه بيضه فالحدأة تحضنها، فإذا فرخت فالحدأة الذكر تعجب من ذلك، ولا يزال يزعق ويضرب الأنثى حتى يقتلها، ينظر: «حياة الحيوان» (۱ : ۲۲۹)، و عجائب المخلوقات (۲) : (۲۵۹)، و(المعجم الوسيط ( ص ١٥٩).
Book of Hunting / Its Conditions and Rulings. Then know that the pig is excluded because it is impure in essence. Abu Yusuf excluded the lion due to its high spirit, and the bear due to its baseness. Some added the kite (a bird of prey) to it due to its baseness. The apparent view is that there is no need for exception because the lion and bear cannot be trained due to high spirit and baseness, so the condition for lawful hunting is not met. 1. That the sending (of the animal) occurs. 2. That the hunter be of those who slaughter, i.e., Muslim or Kitabi. 3. That no one whose hunting is not lawful, like a Magian, shares in the sending. 4. That he does not omit the tasmiyah intentionally. He expressed these four by saying: 'And the sending of a Muslim, both of them, while naming (Allah).' The fifth: That he does not engage in another action between the sending and taking. He expressed it by saying: 'And that he does not sit back from seeking it... etc.' Five conditions in the game: First: That it prevents itself from being approached, either by its legs or wings, thus excluding domesticated animals. He expressed it by saying: 'On a wild game that is inaccessible.' Second: That it is not from insects. Third: That it is not from sea animals, except fish. Fourth: That it is not a predator or one that hunts with its fangs or claws. He expressed these three by saying: 'It is eaten because nothing of them is eaten.' Fifth: That it dies (by the hunting) before reaching the point of slaughtering it. I did not find it explicitly in the Matn, although some of its wording alludes to it generally. (1) Kite (with kasra): It is a bird of prey, the basest of birds, overcome by most birds, swoops on rats and domestic animals. The crow steals the kite's eggs and leaves its own in their place, so the kite incubates them. When they hatch, the male kite is amazed, and he keeps screaming and hitting the female until he kills her. See: 'Hayat al-Hayawan' (1:229), 'Aja'ib al-Makhluqat' (2:259), 'Al-Mu'jam al-Wasit' (p. 159).
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school on the conditions for permissible hunting. The author first addresses exceptions: pig is absolutely forbidden because it is najis al-ayn. Abu Yusuf adds lion and bear as exceptions because they cannot be trained (the lion due to high spirit, the bear due to baseness). Some also add the kite bird for its baseness. However, the preferred view (az-zahir) is that no explicit exception is needed—since these animals cannot be trained, the essential condition of ta'lim (training) is absent, so hunting with them is automatically unlawful. The passage then enumerates four conditions for the hunter: (1) sending the animal, (2) being Muslim or Kitabi, (3) no partner who is not a halal hunter (like a Magian), (4) not deliberately omitting tasmiyah. A fifth condition is that the hunter must not engage in a separate action between sending and taking. For the game, five conditions are listed: (1) wild and inaccessible, (2) not insects, (3) not sea creatures except fish, (4) not a predator hunting by fang or claw, (5) the game must die from the hunt before reaching the hunter alive for slaughter. The footnote describes the kite bird in detail, emphasizing its baseness.

Key Terms
ممتنع متوحش (mumtani' mutawahhish): Wild game that is inaccessible, i.e., cannot be caught by hand; excludes domesticated animals.
متعد (muta'addī): A predator that attacks with fangs or claws; such animals are not lawful to hunt.
تسمية (tasmiyah): The utterance of 'Bismillah' upon sending the hunting animal.
مُعلِّم (mu'allim): A trained hunting animal that returns when called and does not eat the prey.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for the game (the hunted animal) to be lawful?
A) The game must be wild and inaccessible.
B) The game must not be an insect.
C) The game must be from sea animals, including fish.
D) The game must die from the hunt before being slaughtered.

Key Takeaway
Hunting is only halal when five conditions apply to the hunter, five to the game, and certain animals (pig, lion, bear) are absolutely excluded either by impurity or untrainability.

The Condition of Wounding in Hunting: Imām Abū Yūsuf’s Position
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wounding in hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute over the requirement of wounding (jarh) in hunting and apply the practical ruling when wounding is absent.

Core Idea
Wounding is required by Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad; Abū Yūsuf holds it is not a condition.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāya presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement over whether the hunting animal must inflict a wound (jarh) for the game to be lawful. While the majority of the school demands a visible wound, Abū Yūsuf takes a more flexible view. The commentary by al-Akmal and Jalabī shows that even in the majority view, practical tolerance is applied.

Guided Path
1
State the positions
Identify the ruling of Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad (wounding required) and Abū Yūsuf (wounding not required).
The passage explicitly contrasts both views.
2
Understand the basis
The requirement of wounding is derived from the condition that the hunting animal be trained (muʿallam); if it kills without wounding, the kill may be attributed to the animal's action or to the hunter's non-wounding act.
Abū Yūsuf argues that the death itself is sufficient.
3
Examine the tolerance
Al-Akmal notes that even if a condition is missing, the hunt is not automatically haram. Consider cases where the hunter catches the game alive and slaughters it, or the game does not die but is later slaughtered.
This tolerance is part of the muftā bihī approach.
4
Apply the muftā bihī
The reliant position is that wounding is a condition, but relaxation is given when slaughter occurs. Thus, if the game is caught alive and slaughtered, it is halal.
This balances the majority view with practical flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بشرط : عليهما، وجرحهما : أي موضع منه
(بشرط عليهما ) ، وجرحهما (۳) : أي موضع منه) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له : ومحمد ه ، وعن أبي يوسف : أنه لا يشترط الجرح
قال الأكمل بعد نقل هذه الشروط من نهاية الخلاصة»: وفيه تسامح ؛ لأنَّ هذه شرائط الاصطياد للأكل بالكلب لا غير، على أنه لو انتفى بعضه لم يحرم، كما لو اشتغل بعمل لكن أدركه حياً ،فذبحه، وكذا إذا لم يمت بهذا، لكنه ذبحه، فإنه صيد ، وهو حلال، هذا خلاصه ما قال أخي جلبي في «حاشيته»(۳) فليتأمل فيه.
Condition: upon both of them, and their wounding: i.e., a place from it. (Condition: upon both of them, and their wounding (3): i.e., a place from it). This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And from Abu Yusuf: that wounding is not required. Al-Akmal said after quoting these conditions from 'Nihayat al-Khulasa': 'There is tolerance in this, because these are conditions for hunting for eating with a dog only. Even if one of them is missing, it is not unlawful, as if he engaged in another action but caught it alive and slaughtered it; similarly if it did not die from this, but he slaughtered it, then it is game and it is halal.' This is the summary of what my brother Jalabi said in his marginalia, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the necessity of wounding (jarh) in hunting. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, a condition for valid hunting is that the trained animal (dog or bird) must wound the game, i.e., cause a wound that indicates the kill is by the animal. They require that the wound be in any part of the body. Abu Yusuf, however, holds that wounding is not a condition; the essential thing is that the game dies from the animal's act, even if there is no visible wound. The commentator Al-Akmal notes that the strictness is tempered: even if a condition is missing, the hunt is not automatically haram. For example, if the hunter engages in another action but catches the game alive and slaughters it properly, it is halal. Similarly, if the game does not die from the animal but is later slaughtered, it is still permissible game. This shows that the conditions are ideal but not absolute.

Key Terms
جرح (jarh): Wounding: the requirement that the hunting animal inflicts a wound on the game as part of the kill.
اصطياد (istiyad): Hunting: the act of capturing wild game by means of a trained animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wounding is required and the game must die from it
B) Wounding is not a condition; the essential is the death caused by the animal
C) Wounding is required only for birds of prey
D) Wounding is encouraged but not obligatory

Key Takeaway
The majority requires wounding, but Abū Yūsuf's view and practical tolerance ensure flexibility.
