Definition and Conditions of Training Hunting Animals
Definition of training for hunting dogs and birds of prey

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ta'lim for hunting dogs and birds of prey, and explain the conditions that establish training.

Core Idea
A dog is trained after three refusals to eat; a hawk is trained when it returns when called.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines the legal concept of training (ta'lim) for hunting animals. It distinguishes between fanged animals (dogs) and clawed animals (birds of prey), each with its own criterion. Understanding this definition is essential for determining the permissibility of consuming game caught by these animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of hunting animals
The text divides hunting animals into dhu nab (fanged, e.g., dogs) and dhu mikhleb (clawed, e.g., hawks). Recognize that each has a different training test.
Dogs are the primary fanged animal; wolves are also included but less common.
2
For dogs: understand the training test
Training is established when the dog is sent three times and each time it kills the prey but does not eat it. This is explicitly stated from Imam Muhammad's al-Asl.
The dog must refrain from eating three consecutive times; fewer times may be due to satiety.
3
For hawks: understand the training test
Training is shown when the hawk returns when called. This is based on a narration from Ibn Abbas and the nature of hawks being wild and averse to humans.
Hawks are not beaten like dogs because their bodies cannot endure it.
4
Grasp the rationale behind the different tests
Dogs are naturally accustomed to plundering, so the sign of training is leaving their habit (eating). Hawks are naturally wild, so responding to the call indicates training.
The number three for dogs eliminates coincidence; less than three could be due to fullness.
5
Appreciate the legal significance of this definition
The definition is used in many rulings in Hanafi fiqh. A hunting animal that meets these criteria is considered trained, and its catch is lawful to consume.
Other schools may have different criteria; this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : علّمهما ؛ أي بشرط تعليمها ؛ أي ذي ناب وذي مخلب ، أما تعليم ذي ناب من كلب أن يترك الأكل ثلاث مرات.
قال في كتاب «الأصل» : إذا أخذ الصيد ولم يأكل ، وأخذ الآخر ولم يأكل، ثم صاد الثالث ولم يأكل فهو مُعلّم، والتعليم عندنا أن يرسل ثلاث مرات كل مرة بقتل الصيد ولم يأكل. وأما تعليم ذي مخلب من بازي أن يرجع ويجب إذا دعوته، وهو مأثور عن ابن عباس له ؛ ولأن بدن البازي لا يحتمل الضرب، وبدنُ الكلب يحتمله فيضرب ؛ ليتركه ؛ ولأنَّ آية التعليم ترك ما هو ألوفةٌ عادةً، والبازي متوحش متنفر، فكانت الإجابة آية تعليمه. أما الكلب فهو مألوف يعتاد الانتهاب، فكان آية تعليمه ترك مألوفه : وهو الأكل والاستلاب، وإنما شرط ثلاثاً ؛ لأنَّ فيما دونها مزيد الاحتمال، فلعله ترك مرة أو مرتين شبعاً ، فإذا تركه ثلاثاً دل على أنه صار عادة له، وهذا التقدير معتبر في مواضع عديدة (1)
His saying: 'ta'llamahuma' i.e., by the condition of training them; i.e., a fanged (animal) and a clawed (animal). As for training a fanged one like a dog: that it leaves (the prey) without eating it three times. He said in the book 'al-Asl': If it takes the game and does not eat, and takes another and does not eat, then catches a third and does not eat, then it is trained. Training according to us is that it is sent three times each time killing the game and not eating. As for training a clawed one like a hawk: that it returns and responds when called. This is narrated from Ibn Abbas. Because the hawk's body cannot bear beating, while the dog's body can bear it, so it is beaten to make it leave. And because the sign of training is leaving what is natural habit. The hawk is wild and averse, so responding is the sign of its training. As for the dog, it is familiar and accustomed to plundering, so the sign of its training is leaving its habit: eating and snatching. The condition of three times is because less than that has more possibility; perhaps it left once or twice due to being full. So if it leaves three times, it indicates that it has become a habit for it. This estimation is considered in many places.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage defines the legal concept of ta'lim (training) for hunting animals, distinguishing between fanged animals (dogs) and clawed animals (birds of prey like hawks). For a dog, training is established when it refuses to eat the prey three consecutive times when sent. This is from the book 'al-Asl' of Imam Muhammad: catching three game animals without eating proves training. For a hawk, training is shown by its returning when called, because it is naturally wild and its body cannot endure beating. The dog, being tame and inclined to snatch, is trained by beating to leave its habit of eating. The number three is specified to eliminate doubt: fewer times might be due to satiety, but three shows a consistent habit. This definition is rooted in the Hanafi school and is used in many rulings.

Key Terms
تعليم (ta'lim): Training: the process of conditioning a hunting animal to obey commands and refrain from eating the prey.
ذو ناب (dhu nab): Fanged animal: typically refers to dogs used in hunting.
ذو مخلب (dhu mikhleb): Clawed animal: birds of prey like hawks, falcons, and eagles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If it returns when called
B) If it does not eat the prey three separate times when sent
C) If it refrains from eating once
D) If it chases the prey without killing

Key Takeaway
A hunting dog is trained after three refusals to eat; a hawk is trained when it returns when called.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Wound Condition in Hunting Dogs
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wound condition for hunting dogs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf on the necessity of wounding in hunting dogs and explain the basis for the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Whether a hunting dog must wound the prey is disputed among Hanafi imams—Abu Yusuf grants leniency.

This passage examines a specific intra-Hanafi difference: is a wound from the dog a condition for the prey to be halal? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani say yes; Abu Yusuf says no. Later commentator al-Akmal adds flexibility, arguing that even if some conditions are absent, the prey may still be lawful. Understanding this dispute helps in applying Hanafi fiqh to real hunting scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed condition
The passage focuses on the condition that the dog must cause a wound (jarh) on the prey. Determine what exactly is being contested.
The condition is about the dog causing a visible wound; if the dog kills without a wound, it may still be problematic.
2
State the majority position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require the dog to wound the prey. This is recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is the more strict position.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf does not require the wound. He holds that other conditions suffice.
This is a more lenient view.
4
Understand al-Akmal's critique
Al-Akmal quotes from Nihayat al-Khulasah that these conditions are not absolute; if the dog catches the prey alive and the hunter slaughters it, or if the dog does not kill, the meat is halal.
This adds nuance: missing wound does not automatically make the prey haram.
5
Consider the practical consequence
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa/Muhammad, a wound is necessary; under Abu Yusuf, if the dog catches without wounding but the hunter slaughters, it is halal.
This affects fatwa in cases where the dog does not wound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بشرط عليهما ) ، وجرحهما : أي موضع منه) هذا عند أبي حنيفة له : ومحمد ه ، وعن أبي يوسف : أنه لا يشترط الجرح. قال الأكمل بعد نقل هذه الشروط من نهاية الخلاصة»: وفيه تسامح ؛ لأنَّ هذه شرائط الاصطياد للأكل بالكلب لا غير، على أنه لو انتفى بعضه لم يحرم، كما لو اشتغل بعمل لكن أدركه حياً ،فذبحه، وكذا إذا لم يمت بهذا، لكنه ذبحه، فإنه صيد ، وهو حلال، هذا خلاصه ما قال أخي جلبي في «حاشيته» فليتأمل فيه.
(Condition on both of them), and their wound: meaning any place on them. This according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And from Abu Yusuf: that the wound is not required. Al-Akmal said after quoting these conditions from Nihayat al-Khulasah: 'There is leniency in this; because these conditions are for hunting for consumption by a dog only. However, if some of them are absent, it is not forbidden, just as if it busied itself with work, but he caught it alive and slaughtered it. Similarly, if it did not die by this, but he slaughtered it, then it is game and it is lawful. This is the summary of what my brother Chalabi said in his Hashiyah. So let one ponder over it.'
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage opens with a discussion of an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the condition that the hunting dog must cause a wound on the prey. The text states that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require the dog to wound the prey, while Abu Yusuf does not require this. Then al-Akmal is quoted criticizing the inclusion of this condition, arguing that these conditions are specific to hunting with a dog for consumption, and their absence does not automatically make the prey unlawful. He gives examples: if the dog catches the prey alive and the hunter slaughters it, it is still halal; or if the dog kills without wounding? Actually the example is: if the dog did not cause the death but the hunter slaughters it, it is considered game and halal. The passage concludes by saying this is the summary of Chalabi's comment and advises reflection.

Key Terms
جرح (jarh): A wound caused by the dog on the prey; according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it is a condition for the validity of hunting.
تسامح (tasamuh): Leniency or flexibility in applying conditions; al-Akmal says the conditions are not strict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
